[Black journalism directed toward Black readers was a crucial part of
Ida B. Wells' writing]{.underline}. She is a key figure in the
discussion of Black journalism, its directive, and its popularization
amongst Black readers specifically. She wanted her writing to be
accessible to Black audiences: \"I wrote in a plain common-sense way on
the things which concerned our people. Knowing that their education was
limited, I never used a word of two syllables where one would serve the
purpose.\" Her [writing was widely circulated and read by Black
audiences because the white press (mainstream press) didn't see Black
people as an audience]{.underline}. [So, for the most part, the \"white
press ignored Black people unless they were committing crimes or being
lynched,\" which further demonstrates how the press continued to shape
**the narrative around lynching and manipulate the public's perception
of anti-black violence, contributing to its
normalization**.]{.underline} [Ida B. Wells\'s investigative journalism
was a vital part of documenting lynching in an era when government
officials were not only complicit in lynching but also the fact that the
practice was severely under-reported]{.underline}. Her journalism also
worked with modern innovations, especially the use of statistics. Wells,
in her own investigation, created what we would refer to today as
spreadsheets to document lynchings. She would then \"aggregate all the
cases of lynchings that she could find in the newspapers\" and compare
that to the data that she collected.105 Her research ultimately showed
that the narrative of Black men as predators of white women, which
circulated in white newspapers, was a false justification for lynching.
[Wells' personal experience with lynching also revealed that this was a
lie, that lynching was white terrorism.]{.underline}

Southern Horrors investigated 728 lynchings in the South. Through her
investigative research, Wells found that only a third of lynching
victims had been charged with rape, \"to say nothing of those of that
one-third who were innocent of the charge.\"107 Southern horrors also
revealed the refusal of white people to hold white rapists accountable
for the crimes that she investigated related to the rapes, murders, and
lynchings of Black women and girls. Ida B. Wells broke boundaries with
her journalism because she was the only person discussing rape and
interracial sexuality during this period.108 [Ida B. Wells was a
controversial investigative journalist because of the subject matter,
but ultimately a pioneer that must be included in discussions of the
circulation of Black journalism. In her newspaper articles, she would
often call for Black people to boycott racist institutions and stores,
and after the lynchings of her friends, for Black people to abandon the
city of Memphis.]{.underline} She was further determined to abandon
Memphis after a white mob destroyed her newspaper office and threatened
to kill her.109 Ida B. Wells relocated to Chicago for her own safety,
but she continued to protest injustice and write. She published A Red
Record. Tabulated Statistics and Alleged Causes of Lynchings in the
United States, 1892-1893-1894 making the argument that the resurgence of
white mob violence was directly related to Black people gaining
positions of power. [Black press for Black people has always been a site
of protest and activism, and as we move into digital modes of journalism
in the present, we can begin to articulate a connection between the two
while also observing the limitations of agency in Black digital
enclaves.]{.underline}

[The Black press is also connected to postmemory, both emerging as a
response to white supremacist violence and terrorism. It is an
unfortunate reality of the Black experience to live under the constant
threat of racial violence, which has been carefully documented and
committed to memory by Black people. Postmemory is crucial in navigating
and interpreting these moments of violence, while it also functions as a
tool to critique and respond to that violence.]{.underline} [It \"makes
visible what has been obscured, what has been forgotten by white media
and their specific renderings of history]{.underline}\"110: [Critical
black memory emerges out of and is motivated by both survival---the
continued ability to struggle and the faith that such struggle will
secure a brighter future---and failure---the persistence of peril and
renewed forms of racial inequity and subjugation. On the one hand,
memory offers a space to organize for the future while making a critical
assessment of the past and present]{.underline}.

For African Americans, in particular, who have traditionally been denied
access to formal historical institutions, memory has been more than a
mere repository of experience. [Postmemory]{.underline} has challenged
official stories and given voice to historical silences. It has
attempted to suture continuity in the face of rupture and fragmentation.
It [has proffered futurity]{.underline} woven [out of]{.underline} the
ineffable terror of [the past. The importance of postmemory is that it
also provides a space for the Black community to organize and learn from
these inevitable and connected instances of violence.]{.underline}
Despite the shared reality that Black life exists under the continued
threat of racially inflicted violence, [it has also produced a culture
of communal mourning for Black people.]{.underline} A pivotal moment
within postmemory that marks a moment of communal mourning is the murder
and public funeral of Emmett Till. [Images from that funeral were
printed and circulated around the world, revealing what white men did to
Black children in the American south.]{.underline}

In 1955 both John H. Johnson's Jet magazine and Robert Abbot's The
Chicago Defender printed the infamous image of Emmett Till's brutalized
face in his casket. [Black journalism was essential in informing
critical masses of Black people about the violence that Till had
succumbed to, the pain that led Mamie Till Mobley to \"let the people
see what they had done . . .\" and, ultimately, the not guilty
verdict.]{.underline} While white media outlets did a report on the
trial of Emmett Till's murderers, it was solely the Black press at the
time that printed and circulated the photos of Emmett Till from his
public funeral in Chicago. [Emmett Till's murder marks a crucial moment
in the history of image circulation]{.underline}. It was the first issue
of Jet magazine that had necessitated a reprint, marking a moment in
which Till's photo and story had gone proto-viral. The historian Elliot
J. Gorn discusses censorship in mainstream media and their refusal to
show the image in newspapers and on the news.

[The Chicago Defender and Jet shared the images because they presented
the painful reality of American racism. They were fulfilling their duty
to the Black community and respecting the wishes of Mamie Till Mobley to
bear witness and to transform their readers into witnesses as
well.]{.underline} [Black journalism was how the Black community
accessed the truth]{.underline}.117 While seventy-seven percent of
American households owned a television at the time, Black journalism was
the primary mode of dissemination of Emmett Till's story and primarily
aided in the circulation of the image of his face. Print media was
accessible, affordable, and easily shared. It could be read aloud in
schools, and it could be further shared and spread via word of mouth.
[The news of Emmett Till's death spread throughout Black communities,
and his story ultimately galvanized Black people to take action and join
the Civil Rights Movement.]{.underline} [His story made their worst
fears tangible; that this could happen to their children, to their loved
ones, and they would be powerless.]{.underline} However, the refusal of
the press to share the images (within the South specifically) was in
response to Mamie Till Mobley\'s decision to share these images of her
son. The white south was infuriated, and they lashed out using the
press.

Communications scholar Davis Houck mapped out the crucial role of the
press in the exoneration of Till's murderers. The first five days after
the trial, the local press was sympathetic, and a local paper stated
that Emmett Till's murder \"dealt the reputation of the South and
Mississippi a savage blow.\" The press was strategic in its
representation of the defendants and of Carolyn Bryant, who was pictured
in a beauty pageant photo. Her husband, Roy Bryant, and his accomplice,
J.W. Millam, were pictured in their military uniforms, further pushing a
visual narrative that they were upstanding, law-abiding citizens and
incapable of committing such a crime. In contradistinction, Emmett Till
was represented as a \"large\" and \"husky\" arrogant young man from the
North. The Delta Democratic Times chastised the NAACP, as well as the
\"friends\" and family of Emmett Till.

The Democratic Times was considered a moderate paper, and their
journalism heavily influenced the local white population, so much so
that three key witnesses in the trial fled the state of Mississippi.120
[The press had immense power when it came to shaping public perception
of the trial, but it wasn't simply their words that wielded all of the
power.]{.underline} The optics of Bryant and Millam as men who had
served America and the dissemination of this narrative were used to
manipulate and distort the public's perception of Emmett Till.121 [The
process by which the white press invoked a historic myth speaks to the
convoluted path that Black people have too often faced in instances of
state and extrajudicial violence.]{.underline} At first appearance,
these racialized myths are presented as \"natural,\" as having always
existed, despite the fact that they are used to present a historical
intention as a natural justification or \"making contingency appear
eternal.\"122 This justification for Till's death in the Southern press
further contributes to the distorted perception of Black people and, in
this context, Black men (and boys). [The white press created a
reactionary backlash against the photo of Emmett Till as an attempt to
neutralize the truth that Emmett Till's photo told. This separation in
the way that the press decided to report on Emmett Till's
kidnapping/murder and the subsequent trial and acquittal further
positioned Black journalism as a site of real truth by and for the Black
community.]{.underline}

### AT: Psychoanalysis non-falsifiable

#### Falsifiability is a bad frame developed to intentionally exclude and refuse to engage theories which western scientists considered unworthy, like post-colonialism. 

**Castellano 12** \[Daniel J. Castellano, SB in Mathematics and Physics
from MIT, former graduate research and teaching fellow in Physics at
Boston University, MA in History from Boston University, specializing in
late medieval and early modern (13th-18th c.) European history, with
emphasis on intellectual and social history. He is the author of From
Medieval to Modern Man., \"The Insufficiency of Empiricism: Part II\",
05/12/2012, No Publication,
https://www.arcaneknowledge.org/philtheo/empiricism2.htm\] // Micah

[The successful use of controlled experiments in science to corroborate
or falsify hypotheses has led many to consider this testing process as
the defining characteristic of scientific inquiry.]{.underline} Sir Karl
[Popper articulated this]{.underline} criterion [when trying to find a
way to distinguish apparent pseudosciences such as Freudianism and
Marxism]{.underline} from genuine science. [Popper asserted]{.underline}
that [a truly scientific theory must be "falsifiable,"]{.underline} that
is to say, it can be subjected to a controlled experiment that could
conceivably contradict it. Conversely, [any theory]{.underline} that
does [not]{.underline} admit the possibility of [empirical falsification
is regarded as pseudoscience.]{.underline}

The falsifiability standard has attained widespread acceptance among
scientists, though [philosophers of science have pointed out several
problems with such a definition of scientific method.]{.underline}

First, the [falsifiability]{.underline} standard [was]{.underline}
[**chosen by Popper deliberately to exclude theories he intuitively
judged unscientific, so it** possibly]{.underline} [**contains**
more]{.underline} **[cultural bias]{.underline}** [than an objective
theory of knowledge should]{.underline}. Second**[, it is not clear that
this standard includes or excludes the theories that Popper
intended]{.underline}**. Some Marxists and Freudians are quite amenable
to refutation by experiment, while [some mathematical theories of
physics deal with particle interactions that are fundamentally
unobservable]{.underline}. Moreover, [Popper's definition might be
interpreted to exclude mathematics and many of the social
sciences]{.underline}, particularly those studying the unrepeatable
past. [Falsifiability might be a good standard for empirical natural
science, but not science in the broader, classical sense of the
term.]{.underline}

So-called pseudosciences such as Freudianism, Marxism, and astrology do
not meet the falsifiability standard, to the extent that their defenders
resort to special pleading to explain away failed predictions, rather
than admit a failure of their theory. This seems to make the scientific
or pseudoscientific status of a theory depend more on the behavior of
its adherents than on any intrinsic characteristic of the theory as
such.

[Tautological knowledge]{.underline}, which may be deduced by
philosophers and mathematicians, **[would seem to be inherently
unfalsifiable.]{.underline}** This exclusion reminds us that [a theory
of empirical science can never serve as a general theory of knowledge,
as there are other possible paths to knowledge.]{.underline} Thus
pseudosciences that do not meet the falsifiability standard are not
thereby discredited in the least. All that is proven is that they are
not empirical sciences of nature, but neither are mathematics and
philosophy, and that is not to their discredit.

The [falsifiability]{.underline} standard [counterintuitively suggests
that the credibility of a scientific theory is derived from the
possibility of it being wrong.]{.underline} It is [more
accurate]{.underline} to assert that [a scientific theory gains
credibility from its *verifiability*]{.underline}, by successfully
[passing tests]{.underline} where it might have been proven wrong. What
is paramount is that a theory is consistent with observation, and this
has been the hallmark of physical science for the last three centuries,
without explicit reference to a falsifiability standard. [As long as a
theory is confirmed]{.underline} by controlled experiment, the
hypothetical [possibility of a negative result is of secondary
importance]{.underline}.

Equally counterintuitive is the implication that a theory lacking
falsifiability loses credibility. Intuitively, [if a proposition is
absolutely not falsifiable, it is certainly true]{.underline}, though it
may be merely a tautology. Regarding pseudosciences as non-falsifiable
gives them too much credit, when in fact their excuses for failed
predictions can be refuted by evidence and argument. All too often the
[falsifiability]{.underline} standard is used as **[an excuse to refuse
to engage a theory]{.underline}**, maintaining that its exponents will
not accept any refutation.

Since falsifiability really means being *empirically* falsifiable, and
the empirical is restricted to physical observation, Popper's theory
furtively incorporates philosophical materialism into his theory of
science, which is then misused as a general theory of knowledge. With
one stroke, any sort of metaphysical, religious, or spiritual
speculation is dismissed as not meriting credibility. Rather than be
forced to honestly engage metaphysical arguments with counterarguments,
we are excused from debating them altogether, as if they were beyond
reason. Clearly, this position is unwarranted, and it arises from the
error of equating non-empiricism with irrationality.

### AT: Extinction Outweighs/Util

#### Utilitarian calculations of risk are [unethical]{.underline} and justify [existentially genocidal violence]{.underline}.

**Oliver, 23**---Department of Philosophy, Vanderbilt University (Kelly,
"The Limits and Dangers of Risk-Benefit Analysis: From the Refugee
Crisis to the Coronavirus Pandemic," *The Vulnerability of the Human
World: Well-being, Health, Technology and the Environment*, Chapter 2,
pp. 13--27, SpringerLink, dml)

In Carceral Humanitarianism (2017), I argue against [utilitarian
risk-benefit calculations]{.underline} as a principle of ethics. There,
I argue that the utilitarian calculus used by both humanitarian aid
organizations and so-called humanitarian military operations (with its
surgical strikes and statistical modelling) [**reduces** human life to
**statistical calculations** of risk or benefit]{.underline}---and
**[not]{.underline}** just risk or benefit [in the
**absolute**]{.underline}, [but]{.underline} rather risk or benefit as
defined by the organization or government doing the calculations. Risks
and benefits are [**always** defined with certain goals in
mind]{.underline}. [They are **never** without context]{.underline},
[and]{.underline} therefore [purely]{.underline}
"**[objective]{.underline}**" [or **free** from]{.underline} politics or
**[bias]{.underline}**. Thus, any utilitarian calculus assumes some good
or goal beyond calculation such as pain or pleasure, suffering or
quality of life. [Even **survival**]{.underline}---life and death---[are
valued relative to particular goals that **defy** the calculations of
risk-benefit analysis]{.underline}. In other words, employing
risk-benefit and utilitarian calculations, requires making assumptions
about end goals and values that defy those very calculations. It is not
just that [there are **always** factors beyond our control and variables
we **do not know**]{.underline}, as the deadly coronavirus makes
painfully clear, but also that risk-benefit and [utilitarian
calculations do not operate in a **vacuum** but **necessarily** make
assumptions about the good]{.underline}.

In the West, risk-benefit analysis or [utilitarian calculative
reasoning]{.underline} has become the dominant mode of addressing social
issues, economic issues, and health issues. For example, Doctors without
Borders uses complex risk-benefit computer modeling to set policies
about where to send medical aid (Neuman, 2016).Footnote 9 And too many
hospitals dealing with the Covid-19 pandemic have had to resort to
triage medicine that uses risk-benefit analysis to distribute scarce
resources such as ICU beds, oxygen, and ventilators.

This type of calculative reasoning, however, [treats human life as
**fungible** and **exchangeable**]{.underline}, [and]{.underline}
thereby [makes acceptable]{.underline} so-called [**collateral damage**
and **death**]{.underline}. The way risk-benefit analysis is used to
calculate acceptable collateral damage in warfare is a prime example of
the costs of calculable reason, where utilitarian arguments are used to
justify acceptable levels of killing and death. [The calculating machine
at the heart of utilitarian risk-benefit analysis is **dangerous** when
it makes individual lives fungible and exchangeable]{.underline}.
[This]{.underline} approach [**does not** and **cannot** address the
**singularity** of each living being]{.underline}.

Both humanitarian aid and "humanitarian" warfare operate according to
logics of calculation. In fact, humanitarian warfare and humanitarian
aid operate in tandem. In a sense, nation states create refugees by
limiting citizenship, and then pay humanitarian aid organizations to
take care of them. National sovereignty and its concomitant notion of
citizenship are dependent upon so-called neutral humanitarian space,
which occupies an ambiguous space on the outskirts, and borders of, host
countries. Refugees are detained in camps and detention centers at the
borders in supposedly neutral humanitarian space free from politics and
war. Yet, this neutral humanitarian space, often guarded by state police
and military, is far from independent of state sovereignty.

To the contrary, humanitarian space is coopted to maintain national
sovereignty against the so-called threat of migrants, asylum seekers,
and refugees. In other words, within carceral humanitarianism, refugees
operate as both a threat to national sovereignty, and the justification
for shoring up borders.

The fact that the United States and the European Union pay other
countries to keep refugees from crossing into their borders makes clear
that the lives of these people can be bought and sold like so much cargo
to be held in port or lost at sea. For example, the recent crisis in
Belarus at the Polish border, where, according to migrants and refugees
are being used as political pawns. According to the New York Times:
"Europe has long paid other nations to keep refugees away from its
borders... This gives countries on the European periphery the leverage,
and incentive, to use those refugees as pawns" (see Fisher,
2021).Footnote 10

Policies that treat refugees as collateral damage or units of exchange,
where some lives are more valuable than others as calculated through
risk-benefit analysis, renders the lives of refugees fungible, and
therefore, ultimately, disposable.

Statistical models that compute [comparative valuations set out a
**hierarchy** of life]{.underline}, human and otherwise, [through which
violence is **justified**]{.underline}, [and some lives are
**valuable**]{.underline} (in this case, citizens), [while others are
**disposable**]{.underline} (in this case, refugees). Yet, [in **any**
risk-benefit or utilitarian calculation]{.underline}, [there is
**always** a remainder that **cannot be calculated**]{.underline}---not
just in the arithmetic sense, but also [a remainder beyond calculation
and beyond recognition]{.underline}.

[There is always something we **don't know** that is **necessarily left
out** of any calculation]{.underline}. [We are **not masters** of the
world and therefore there are always factors **beyond** our knowledge
and control]{.underline}. In addition, there are elements beyond our
comprehension. [It is **not**]{.underline}, then, [just a matter of
**gathering all the relevant data**]{.underline}, and that fact that we
always fall short. [There are elements that affect our calculations that
we **aren't even aware of**]{.underline}, [that we **can never
know**]{.underline}. [There will always be something **outside our
comprehension affecting our calculations**]{.underline}. Furthermore,
[human experience]{.underline}, and the experience of life itself,
[**cannot be reduced** to calculations or exchangeable
units]{.underline}. [There is always something about life that
**transcends** even the most sophisticated mathematical
calculations]{.underline}. We might call this the remainder left over
when we attempt to translate ethics into politics or the singular into
the universal. There is always something about the singularity of each
living being that cannot be reduced to any system of exchange value.

2.3 Justice Between Ethics and Politics

Justice requires continually renegotiating the relationship between
ethics and politics, singular and universal, precisely because there is
always something in excess, something beyond recognition.
[This]{.underline} remainder [is the **ethical imperative** that should
**keep us awake at night**]{.underline}. [Rather than lead to
**inquietude** or **apathy**]{.underline}, the excess beyond our
[recognition keeps us **actively searching** for better ways to approach
justice **without** resting on]{.underline} moral rules or [risk-benefit
calculations]{.underline} or self-righteous principles. The remainder
beyond calculation, beyond recognition, demands that we continue to
question our own principles and policies, that we continue to examine
our concepts of justice and fairness and equality.

An [ethical politics]{.underline} is one in which justice demands the
hyper-vigilance self-critical reflection on where any response---ethical
or political---necessarily falls short. It is an ongoing task that
[**cannot end** with risk-benefit analysis]{.underline}. [Despite the
**ethical insomnia** and **political vertigo** caused by this
interminable self-questioning]{.underline}, [we must
**act**]{.underline}---[even **leap**]{.underline}. Although ultimately
the gap between singularity and universality is impassible, [the
impossibility of achieving justice is **no excuse** for not **attempting
it every single day**]{.underline}.

This tension between politics (which addresses itself to what can be
seen, and to the universal, what can be shared), and ethics (which
addressed itself to what cannot be seen, and to the singular, what
cannot be shared) is the knot at the center of justice.

[Justice is **antithetical** to utilitarian ethics insofar as
individuals are **not fungible units** that can be
exchanged]{.underline}. [Utilitarianism relies on the **calculability**
of]{.underline} pain, suffering, **[survival]{.underline}**, [or the
quality of life]{.underline} itself. And the [utilitarian calculus
relies on determining **thresholds** that separate individuals into
groups]{.underline}, whether it is those who are members of the moral
community or the polis, and those who are not (for example animals), or
those [whose lives are **worth saving** and those whose lives are
**not**]{.underline}.

At some point, however, [this type of line-drawing **inevitably**
becomes either **political** or **arbitrary**]{.underline}. Take debates
over the basis for membership in the moral community. What are the
criteria for membership? Intelligence, language, reason, sentience, the
ability to suffer? And [whatever we decide should be the
criteria]{.underline}, [where do we **draw the line**?]{.underline} For
example, what counts as a language? Does the bees signal dance count?
Or, as some argue, is it merely a code and not a language? What counts
as intelligence? Peter Singer argues that great apes should be extended
rights or admitted to the moral community based on their intelligence,
which is greater than severely mentally disabled persons (see Singer,
1999; McMahan, 2009; Anstötz, 1993).Footnote 11 Some disability scholars
such as Eva Kittay have argued against this comparison, insisting that
human beings should be accorded special status regardless of their
mental abilities (Kittay, 1998a, b, 2009).Footnote 12 The controversy
over Singer's argument demonstrates how [utilitarian line-drawing is
always a **fraught endeavor** that ultimately rests on an **arbitrary
cut off point**]{.underline}.

For example, in Animal Liberation, Singer draws the line between calms
and shellfish, who supposedly cannot feel pain (Singer, 1990,
173--4).Footnote 13 But what if we learn that shellfish can feel pain?
In all these utilitarian endeavors, the liminal cases are unsettling
because they reveal the arbitrariness of borders and the inadequacy of
general classifications. This type of line-drawing or threshold not only
ignores the singularity of each individual, but also poses an arbitrary
or political dividing line as a natural border. The same is true of
national borders used to keep migrants out.

We see something similar in covid triage medicine where triage protocols
disadvantage whole classes of people. Old people, disabled people,
cancer survivors. The coronavirus lays bare the fantasy of calculable
criteria as an ethical way of life. Covid reveals gaping inequities in
the length and quality of life caused by social and economic
"pre-existing conditions." Celebrities and politicians from Madonna to
Andrew Cuomo called the coronavirus the "great equalizer." In March
2020, Cuomo tweeted "This virus is the great equalizer." In reality,
covid is the great unequalizer... Footnote 14

The pandemic forces doctors and nurses to make impossible decisions
about who is given the chance to live and who will die. But these
decisions exist in the context of pre-existing conditions of poverty,
racism, and unequal access to health care that skew any attempt at fair
or reasonable calculation. In a fair and just world, there would be
adequate resources available to everyone. Tragically, the real world is
not so fair or just. And [risk-benefit analysis and calculative
reasoning become the justification for **allowing some to
die**]{.underline}. [When those calculations pose as **justice** or
**fairness**]{.underline}, [and when they allow us to feel
**certain**]{.underline}, even righteous, [in our
**decisions**]{.underline}, then [they risk a **slippery slope** that
leads us to a place where **any** and **all life** can become
**disposable**]{.underline}.

The pandemic has made painfully clear the limitations of risk-benefit
analysis and calculative reasoning. Doctors and nurses report being
traumatized when forced to decide who will get life saving resources.
Face-to-face with patients, risk-benefit calculations are not as
straight forward as the mathematical or statistical modelling suggests.

A group of research physicians at the University of California,
describes the problem:

[Utilitarian approaches]{.underline} to ethical problems [often
**appear** to be **more practical** than many alternatives]{.underline}:
[we can **know** what the right course of action is by calculating
whichever choice will produce the **greatest good** for the **greatest
number**]{.underline}. Part of the appeal of utilitarian ethics is that
it seems like it should "work." [In the **real world**]{.underline},
however, [performing the relevant calculations can be **far from
easy**]{.underline}. This is especially true in crisis situations. [It
may be **impossible** to collect the **data** needed]{.underline} as
input for the calculations, it may be difficult or impossible [to create
the **decision rules**]{.underline} (predicting prognosis or comparing
outcomes) that constitute the calculations, [the calculator may be
**impaired** by any number of different factors]{.underline} (moral
distress, fatigue, internal and external pressures, etc.) [or
**non-utilitarian moral constraints**]{.underline} (such as superseding
egalitarian policies, or even exponentially increasing crisis severity)
[can make utilitarian approaches **infeasible**]{.underline}. (Fedyk et
al., 2020)Footnote 15

2.4 The "Lessor of Evils"

[Rather than an ethical solution]{.underline}, [utilitarian risk-benefit
analysis]{.underline} in a triage medicine [is a]{.underline}
"**[lesser]{.underline}** of the **[evils]{.underline}**"
[approach]{.underline}. In fact, as I discovered doing research for
Carceral Humanitarianism, when discussing both humanitarian aid and
warfare, experts described risk-benefit and collateral damage
calculations as the "lessor of the evils" (Oliver, 2017). Some even see
these policies of assessing collateral damage as preventing the worst
evil by preventing massacre, genocide, and mass death. But as Hannah
Arendt reminds us, "[Those who choose the lesser evil **forget very
quickly** that they **still chose evil**]{.underline}" (Arendt, 2003,
30).Footnote 16

In Carceral Humanitarianism, I consider the question of "the worst"
violence. There, I argue that contemporary logics of proportionality
employed by both humanitarian aid organization and humanitarian military
operations are designed to mitigate and control violence and death to
avoid the worst violence and death, according to calculations of the
"lessor of evils." Thus, [we must ask what is the]{.underline} "most
evil," or the [**worst** evil]{.underline}. We may think of genocide as
the worst evil, and, in the West, "the worst" is often shorthand for the
"final solution" and the Holocaust. Since the end of World War II, and
the Cold War, "[the **worst**]{.underline}" [has become associated
with]{.underline} the possibility of nuclear war, even [the destruction
of the **entire planet**]{.underline}.

[What if the worst is a result of the **logic of calculation** taken to
the **extreme** at the expense of **all other worldviews**?]{.underline}
[What if the worst is when this calculable way of thinking is used to
justify **massacre**]{.underline}, **[genocide]{.underline}**, [and
**mass death**?]{.underline} [When lives become
**fungible**]{.underline} and exchange, then [we risk]{.underline} "[the
**worst**]{.underline}." Insofar as contemporary so-called
"humanitarian" warfare operates according to the logic of utilitarian
calculations of collateral damage that make some lives disposable, it
too risks "the worst."

[The worst goes **beyond literal killing** and signals the
**erasure**]{.underline} a way [of]{.underline} seeing the world---or we
might as well say [a **world itself**]{.underline}---[by **covering it
over** with another worldview]{.underline}. In this regard, [comparative
models that **reduce** life to units to be **exchanged** or **plugged
into equations** operate according to a **world-destroying genocidal
logic**]{.underline}. This is to say, when "the worst" becomes part of
the economy of "lessor of evils," life and death are reduced to a logic
of calculation that makes them fungible; one life is weighed against
another. Which is the lesser evil: One thousand four hundred and
twenty-two refugees who died in the Mediterranean Sea in 2020 or the
cost to citizens of the European Union of providing safe passage to
refugees? Which is the lesser evil: killing one civilian infant or
killing three soldiers in a surgical strike? Which is the lessor evil: a
50-year-old diabetic and mother of two with covid dying because she
doesn't have access to a ventilator, or a widowed and otherwise healthy
70-year-old man dying without access to a ventilator? Etc. Etc.

What is an acceptable death? The death as collateral damage? [To be
**calculable**]{.underline}, [each life must be **reducible** to a
common denominator and **exchangeable**]{.underline}. [Each life becomes
a unit in an equation]{.underline}, a risk factor.

Yet, as the examples I just mentioned suggest, [these decisions as to
which is **worse**]{.underline}---[or which is the **lesser** of the
evils]{.underline}---[are **always** made by **privileging one context**
over another]{.underline}. Citizenship over foreign nations. Civilians
over military. Diabetes over motherhood. [How do we weigh one life
against another?]{.underline} [How do we decide whose life is worth
more?]{.underline} Whose life is worth more and whose life is expendable
as collateral damage? [Which factors are deemed relevant]{.underline},
[and which are not?]{.underline} Which goals are deemed worthy, and
which are not? Ultimately, [utilitarian calculus **cannot give us the
answers** to these questions]{.underline}.

#### The climate is haunted by the ghosts of the Eurocene. The European genocide of Turtle Island represents the most significant change in the C02 record in 2,000 years.

[Ice cores]{.underline} taken from places like Antarctica [are among
**the most important archives for understanding past
climates**]{.underline}. As ice accumulates, it creates layers. [When
drilled, these layers reveal a wealth of information, from dust
concentrations that signal global wind patterns to bubbles of ancient
air that disclose the chemical composition of past
atmospheres]{.underline}. The layers closest to the surface are thicker,
allowing scientists to see how individual seasons and storms impacted
climate. The layers furthest from the surface are compacted and have
thinned over time due to glacial dynamics, but [these layers allow
scientists to understand past climates at longer time
scales]{.underline} (Alley 2000). The data taken from these cores [help
reconstruct patterns of past climates; however, this data is also
increasingly used to understand human history.]{.underline} As Antonello
and Carey (2017, 194) note,"[Ice cores are as much about humans as they
are about glaciers and climates, and indeed ice ties people and climate
together closely]{.underline}, in some ways synchronizing them." While

Sheet--show a reduction in atmospheric CO2 of 7-10 parts per million
between 1570 and 1620. These decreases in atmospheric CO2 cor- respond
with what Lewis and Maslin (2015) define as the"Orbis Spike," which
[they argue is a **"golden spike," or start date, for the apparent
Anthropocene. This historical moment in the ice record correlates with
the socioecological aftermath of the arrival of Europeans in the
Caribbean in 1492.**]{.underline} Prior to the arrival of Europeans,
there was an estimated 54--61 million people living in the Americas,

increased carbon sequestration, as approximately 50 million hectares of
farming land was left fallow and regenerated over the course of 100

in atmospheric CO2 is the **most prominent feature**, in terms of both
rate of change and magnitude, in pre-industrial CO2 records **over the
past 2,000 years**]{.underline}." Considering the Orbis Spike not only

possible to see how the same social relations that led to [the
inequalities of the present]{.underline} (e.g., an adherence to
Enlightenment-era rationalization via homo oeconomicus), left unchecked,
[are likely to extend into the future climate]{.underline} (Erickson
2020). Further, [when read through the lens of the inhuman, the
**increased sequestration of carbon** due to increased photosynthesis is
less about vegetative regeneration than it **is about the death of
millions of people never considered human.**]{.underline} Taking [the
obvious presence of the absence of certain human lives in the ice record
into account, I find Gordon's (2008) writing on ghostly matters
relevant]{.underline}. Her work is motivated by a "desire to find a
method of knowledge production and a way of writing that could represent
the damage and the haunting of the historical alternatives and thus
richly conjure, describe, narrate, and explain the liens, the costs, the
forfeits, and the losses of modern systems of abusive powerTROUBLE WITH
MODELING THE HUMAN 393 in their immediacy and worldly significance"
(Gordon 2008, xvii). On [haunting,]{.underline} she (2008, 7--8) writes,
it"[is a constituent of modern social life," and being haunted"draws us
affectively]{.underline}, some- times against our will and always a bit
magically [into the structure of feeling of a reality we come to
experience,]{.underline} not as cold knowledge, but as a transformative
recognition." In the case of the Orbis Spike[, I find the
present-absence of millions of lives ghostly; being **haunted by this
absence** demands a radical recognition of those lives that are
presently absent, meaning that the present climate--its material
composition and its associated impacts on contemporary human life--is
haunted by these past lives]{.underline}. The way [human history is
typically interpolated into the ice record, as a signal for all
humanity, is significant for understanding not only the way a flattened
humanity is then projected into the future,]{.underline} but also to the
ways in which [climate science]{.underline}, and its modes of knowledge
production, [are also haunted]{.underline}. Carey et al.'s (2016)
article on the gendered dimensions of glaciological science highlights
how certain humans have been historically excluded from the production
of knowledge around ice and climate. However, this argument could be
taken a step further to suggest that [the purposeful absenting of
certain human lives is a necessary function of this knowledge
production.]{.underline} Reflecting on the death of Trayvon Martin,
James and Vargas argue that Black death is a predict- able and
constituent part of U.S. democracy. They ask,"What will happen then if
instead of demanding justice we recognized (or at least consider) that
the very notion of justice... produces and requires Black exclusion and
death as normative?" (2014, 193). Following a similar logic with regards
to environmental knowledge production, w[hat if marginalized lives have
been historically excluded as a matter of scientific praxis? Consider
that the father of glaciology,]{.underline} Louis Agassiz, was also the
father of scientific racism in the U.S. (Gordon 2008), or that John
Muir, founder of the conservation organization Sierra Club, [was a white
supremacist]{.underline} (Fears and Mufson 2020). In their analysis of
plastic pollution, Liboiron (2021) articulates how [pollution
science--and its assumptions about land use, contamination, etc.--are
inherently based on colonial ideas of natural relations as universal and
the absenting of Indigenous knowledge systems]{.underline}. When
hindcasting the inhuman, **[the present becomes an amalgamation of
ghosts]{.underline}**. [The present- absence of the millions of lives
lost due to European colonization can be seen in the chemical
composition of our rapidly warming climate]{.underline}. Thinking
through the lens of the inhuman allows for a deeper understanding of the
intersecting materialities and subjectivities of past lives not
considered human and their socioecological relations. However, [as with
hindcasting in climate modeling, hindcasting the inhuman allows for a
reckoning with past inequalities regarding attributions to climate
change and with colonial practices and social relations that have led to
the manifestation of the apparent Anthropocene]{.underline}. With these
necessary calibrations in mind, this paper now turns toward the future.

#### The liquid legacies of blue colonialism and its entanglements with racial enslavement permeate the "post" colonial world. The Anthropocene thus must be understood as the Plantationocene- and any solution to the crisis must stem first from the blue. 

Cydney **Phillip**, The Liquid Plantationocene and Jesmyn Ward's Let Us

Introduction [**The liquid legacies** of transatlantic slavery continue
to spill over into the present, not just figuratively---in the sense
that racial capitalism saturates contemporary ecologies and
economies---but materially, too.]{.underline} [Slavery altered the
natural hydrology of the American South (Silkenat 81). Industrialized
agriculture was dependent not only on precipitation patterns---as in,
rainfall for harvest---but also on drainage systems to facilitate the
control of water.]{.underline} Along the Mississippi River, enslaved
people were deployed to build and maintain [canals and
levees]{.underline} were used to prevent flooding and to assist the
cultivation of crops in low-lying areas across the Mississippi Delta and
Louisiana. However, as Anna Hartnell writes, "In time it would become
apparent that \[. . .\] [man-made levees deprive the soil of the water
and sediment that builds the land \[. . .\] and keeps it from collapsing
in on itself and sinking"]{.underline} (934). In the words of Richard
Campanella, "All this creates air pockets in the soil body, into which
those sand, silt, and clay particles settle, consolidate---and drop
below sea level" (Campanella). Compounded by anthropogenic climate
change and the construction of oil pipelines, [the hydraulic
infrastructures of the Plantation continue to shape environmental crises
across the US south]{.underline}, contributingISLE: Interdisciplinary

I reflect on how the ending of the novel, ultimately, resonates with
histories of "marronage and other forms of fugitive mobilities," which
as Leslie Gross-Wyrtzen and Alex Moulton write, "highlight the social
and spatial dynamics of resistance and refashioning that African,
African-descended, and Indigenous people enacted wherever chattel
slavery existed" (1260). Through the neoslave narrative, Ward salvages
the miscellaneous water-related histories and knowledges that are
bypassed by the limited purview of the Anthropocene (tailored
exclusively to the Western "Anthropos") as well as terrestrial
definitions of the Plantationocene. If, as Lucy Bond argues, [the
Anthropocene induces a "crisis of memory" through the perpetuation of
"narratives that serve to mask the historical connection between
socio-economic and ecological violence]{.underline}" (196-197), then Let
Us Descend complicates that strategic forgetfulness by articulating how
[**the so-called natural environment is physically and metaphorically
soaked in** histories of transatlantic slavery and its liquid
legacies]{.underline}. The liquid Plantationocene is a nod to two
critical interventions in which the concept of liquid(ity) has proven to
be a generative framework for understanding the fluid dynamics of racial
capitalism. The first is Hartnell's work on the experience of "[**liquid
precarity**" in what is now a sinking New Orleans (933). Whereas Zygmunt
Baumon employs the notion of "liquid modernity" to describe the
unfettered flows of global capitalism, Hartnell identifies the
phenomenon of"liquid precarity" that continues to emerge as a result of
the earth's inability to withstand the weight of racial capitalism
(933). As Hartnell reminds, this liquid precarity is evidenced by the
sea's ongoing absorption of the soil in Louisiana. Second, Rinaldo
Walcott's "The Black Aquatic" centers both the metaphorical and material
proponents of liquid. Walcott argues that "Blackness begins in the
context of liquidity---the coffle and the slave ship are its
founding.]{.underline} From historical examples like the ships Amistad
and Zong, we see how [liquidity marks the birth of black possibility
and/or potentialities that come into being. Liquidity, too, has its
opposite: liquidation"]{.underline} (66). In doing so, Walcott draws the
reader's attention to "[the liquidity of people and economies in which
the historical implications of the ship meet the contemporary
liveability of Black]{.underline} peoples' [lives]{.underline}" (66).
Thus, while the concept of [the Blue Plantationocene]{.underline} might
have situated this essay firmly within the field of **[blue
humanities]{.underline}** scholarship, I want to maintain the focus on
liquid that emerges in the work of both Hartnell and Walcott. [Blue
humanities]{.underline} discourse [is usually focused on the
possibilities that emerge when marine science and cultural studies are
combined to challenge anthropocentric understandings of the sea
(]{.underline}Opperman 2-3). However, grappling with the liquid
ecologies of 4 I S L E Downloaded from
https://academic.oup.com/isle/advance-article/doi/10.1093/isle/isaf029/8105628

its lexical associations with both water and capital, is a particularly
useful term when it comes to apprehending the intersections among
aqueous violence, economic exploitation, and racial domination in the
