foregrounds blue resistance.]{.underline}

# AFF Answers

## Theory

### Theory Wrong\-\--Psychoanalysis Wrong\-\--2AC

#### Psychoanalysis is non-falsifiable bunk science -- reject their claims of "western blue desires"

**Järvinen 21** \[Anna Järvinen, PhD, Goldsmiths College, University of
London, "Psychoanalysis: Science or pseudoscience? examining the status
of Freudian theory," The Skeptic, 2021-08-09,
https://www.skeptic.org.uk/2021/07/psychoanalysis-science-or-pseudoscience-examining-the-status-of-freudian-theory/\]
// Micah

Sigmund [Freud]{.underline} (1856-1939) [insisted that]{.underline} his
postulations that are collectively known as [psychoanalysis formed the
basis for the science of psychology.]{.underline} Yet, ever since its
beginnings in the early years of the twentieth century, [there has been
considerable argument as to whether psychoanalysis is even a science at
all. Some claim that Freud's work itself was entirely
**pseudoscientific**]{.underline} in nature, and that later proponents
of his theory have done little to revise this state of affairs. Yet, in
its approximately one hundred years of existence, [psychoanalysis has
had a tremendous influence on Western culture]{.underline}, and in spite
of all the stones slung in its direction, it still has many supporters
around the globe.

Nobel Laureate Sir Peter Medawar (1984) argues that certain, seemingly
'naïve' questions are beyond the power of pure science to answer, such
as those concerning the purpose of human life. He believes that such
'ultimate questions', as well as psychoanalysis with its subject matter
of 'human nature', belong to the 'domain of myth'. Indeed it is
important to note here that several physical scientists dismiss the
social sciences in general as pseudoscientific.

Before assessing psychoanalysis against the criteria of pseudoscience,
and for that matter, those of science, it is important to briefly
consider what psychoanalysis is, and how it is assumed to work. The
diffusion of Freudian theory is fascinating, it having been adopted for
application in such fields as history, political science, literature,
music and the arts. Further, for many laypersons Freudian theory is
synonymous with psychology. Its goals are immensely ambitious, and
theory and practice work hand-in-hand. Nevertheless, psychoanalysis is
primarily grounded on pseudoscientific postulations that are inherently
non-falsifiable.

Central to Freud's theory is the division of the mind into three levels
of consciousness, the first being the conscious. Below this rests the
preconscious, which contains most of a person's memories, and which in
modern psychological language is equivalent to longterm memory. Below
this lies the unconscious, which is one of the key concepts of
psychoanalysis. According to Freud, this contains feelings, desires and
memories that are repressed from the conscious because they are too
traumatic or painful to deal with directly. One example of this putative
repression is the Oedipus complex, which is concerned with incestuous
desires. A further assumption of the unconscious is that the material in
it is not available to the conscious mind, but nevertheless can
powerfully affect behaviour -- it therefore follows that repressed
memories can result in psychological problems.

Sigmund Freud

A major part of Freud's theory deals with personality development,
particularly from the perspective of sexual behaviour and sexual
identity. Freudian theory is divided into four developmental
psycho-sexual stages. In chronological order these are the oral, anal,
phallic and genital stages. In some of these stages one has to resolve
certain 'complexes' in order to develop normally. In Freud's view,
mature personality consists of the id, the ego and the superego, which
all assume very different roles. The id is considered as the very core
of personality, from which the other structures develop. The id is the
most primitive structure, solely seeking to achieve pleasure and avoid
pain. The superego can be thought of as the conscience of the person.
These structures of personality bind together in a complex manner, and
in individuals regarded as 'normal', they function in an interactive
fashion.

Psychologist Terence Hines (1988) proposes several criteria by which a
field may be judged as being either pseudoscientific or otherwise. [One
characteristic of pseudoscientific theories is that they exhibit
non-falsifiable hypotheses]{.underline}. [Some testable predictions can
be derived from psychoanalysis, but they have typically **been shown to
be untrue**]{.underline}. [Several researchers,]{.underline} such as
Hans Eysenck (1985), [have embarked on the experimental study of
Freudian concepts]{.underline} (see also Eysenck & Wilson, 1973). For
instance, Paul Kline (1968) and Calvin Hall (1954) [have empirically
investigated the Freudian developmental assumption that toilet-training
exerts a great influence on personality]{.underline}. This is based on a
postulation that the mother's training practices, together with her
attitudes towards defecation, cleanliness, control and responsibility,
strongly influence the personality development of the child. [If this
training is strict, then the child may take revenge upon authority
figures by being messy, wasteful, extravagant, etc, or alternatively the
child may become excessively neat and meticulous and develop a fear of
dirt and exhibit overcontrolled behaviours]{.underline}. Hence, [the
theory can explain *post hoc* any extent of tidiness or messiness as
resulting from strict toilet-training.]{.underline} According to Hall,
[this theory becomes *twice* non-falsifiable when the effects of gentle
toilet-training are examined --]{.underline} these are indistinguishable
from the result of strict toilet training, and once more, both
personality styles can be explained *post hoc*, but neither can be
predicted. [In addition to the lack of clear logic in Freud's
postulation of the relationship between toilet-training and personality,
studies investigating the relationship between these variables **have
found no relationship** between the actual toilet training practices
employed by parents and the child's resultant personality
style.]{.underline}

Related to these questionable assumptions of [psychoanalysis are two
equally questionable methods of investigating the alleged memories
hidden in the unconscious: free association and the interpretation of
dreams. Neither method is capable of scientific formulation or empirical
testing.]{.underline} These methods bear no validity in reality. When
these results are taken together with others, it seems plausible to
suggest that **[falsifiability poses a problem for most aspects of
psychoanalysis!]{.underline}**

Daisie and Michael Radner (1982), ardent advocates of scientific
methodology, observe that pseudoscientific proponents are inclined to
"look for mysteries". It could certainly be argued that Freud largely
got away with a lack of scientific rigour in his work due to the
volatile, obscure and often inaccessible content of the subject matter
of psychoanalysis, especially as it relates to human sexuality. Whilst
Richard Webster (1995), a radical psychologist, notes that much of
Freud's theory appears elegant and does display internal coherence, he
argues that 'laws' of human nature are largely covered in mystery, and
it is problematic to change this because, in order to investigate 'human
nature', the power of science is not sufficient. Moreover, many of
Freud's achievements are seen to result from his own charismatic
personality and "the heroic myth which he spun around himself during his
own lifetime" (p. 9). Freud is sometimes likened to a religious,
'messianic' figure, who formed a 'church' and obtained a large crowd of
followers, not because of the scientific basis of his teachings, but
solely because of his authority.

Another of Hines' (1988) observations was that pseudoscientists accuse
sceptics of demanding more proof for their postulations in comparison to
that required for more established theorists. But, as Hines puts it,
"extraordinary claims demand extraordinary proof " (p.5). Psychoanalysts
often argue that clinical experiences are the proof for psychoanalytic
theory and thus do not require evidence of the scientific kind, and
these are the facts on which their 'science' is founded. However, since
these data consist of symbolic interpretations of dreams, free
associations, and such, in the light of this discussion such claims may
be seen to be entirely circular in nature. Hines further notes that
pseudoscientific proponents commonly fail to modify their theories in
the light of new evidence. A notable example of this is the idea of
motivated forgetting, also known as repression. Repression is said to be
an active mechanism by which particular memories are prevented from
reaching consciousness due to their emotionally painful content.
Infantile amnesia, referring to the fact that adults usually have
virtually no memories of their infancy, has widely been regarded as
evidence for repression. In Freud's explanation, this is because the
period of early childhood is a time when the child is immersed in strong
sexual desires, which are largely incestuous in nature. Because these
desires cannot be satisfied, frustration follows. For a boy, his sexual
feelings towards his mother may cause castration anxiety. Memories of
these feelings are repressed when the Oedipus complex is resolved. Such
memories cannot be allowed to enter the conscious mind, as there would
be a risk that they would cause perversion and psychological damage. The
[scientific evidence for this notion of unconscious repression is
lacking]{.underline}, [as is any evidence that conscious thought or
behaviour is influenced by repressed memories.]{.underline}

Infantile amnesia is regarded as a real phenomenon, but studies
investigating memory and brain have shown that its causes are far from
the psychoanalytical explanation. The currently accepted explanation is
that infantile amnesia is due to the nature of the brain of the immature
organism. A second important variable in childhood amnesia is the
development of language, i.e., the limited language skills of young
children make it more difficult to memorise information. However, a
large body of evidence showing that infantile amnesia is not due to
repression, but to the immaturity of the infant's brain, has not made
psychoanalysts revise the theory. Another example of a failure to update
psychoanalytic theories is Freud's field of psychohistory, created for
understanding historic individuals. Several flaws in his theorising have
been indicated, but again such theories have not been subsequently
modified.

What is science, then? How scientific is psychoanalysis? According to
Donald Spence (1987), an American psychiatrist, something deserves to be
considered as a science when there is a prevalent regard for the data,
which is available for all interested parties, and when theory is
data-driven and changes in response to new observations. Further, the
progress of the theory is cumulative and the original model may serve as
a basis for newer models. Finally, the claims are based on evidence
rather than on authority. In this view science is immensely democratic.
In terms of the above criteria, psychoanalysis fails on every count.
Frank Sulloway (1979), a psychologist and historian of science, has
actually described psychoanalysis as "a scientific fairytale". However,
the subject matter of psychoanalysis, human sexuality and human nature,
are laden with powerful taboos. Webster (1995) argues that to postulate
theories concerning such issues requires not only intellect, but also
emotional lucidity, something that is not commonly regarded as a
characteristic of the scientific mind. Furthermore, to engage in such an
activity requires a pinch of rebelliousness, which again is a quality
rarely seen amongst those trained in natural sciences. In Webster's
view, scientific ideas should not be judged by their intellectual source
but by their explanatory power.

It cannot be denied, however, that Freud's theory is superlatively
ingenious, creative and perceptive. It further provides an initially
plausible explanation of many aspects of human sexual behaviour. To
dismiss psychoanalysis as being without any intellectual merit or
importance would be wrong, and to do so would dismiss all of the many
respectable authors who ever wrote in the psychoanalytic tradition.

[The majority of psychologists hold that psychoanalysis is a
pseudoscience]{.underline}, but perhaps the very business of examining
Freud's work from the scientific perspective is unnecessarily harsh.
Hans Eysenck, a hard-core behaviourist and iconoclast of the twentieth
century, maintains that for many, Freud's contribution has not been to
the scientific study of human behaviour, but to the interpretation and
meaning of mental events. Specifically, Freud pioneered the desire to
understand those individuals whose behaviour and thinking cross the
bounds of convention dictated by civilization and cultures. It is
difficult to dismiss the importance of psychoanalysis as it is so
tightly entangled with our cultural history. However, perhaps the most
transparent evidence that Freud was wrong is that he is very difficult
for us to understand, and much of him remains an enigma -- in Webster's
(1995, p. 29) words, "a psychologist who is beyond the reach of
psychology, the creator of a movement which has encircled the globe but
which has formulated no theory which can even begin to account for its
own success". [It seems clear to me that **psychoanalysis belongs to the
'domain of myth'**, and while of course myths may be enigmatic, they are
not in the least scientific.]{.underline}

### Theory Wrong\-\--Humanism Good\-\--2AC

#### Humanism is not irredeemable, but rather it is contingent and malleable. The alt jettisons the liberatory potential of humanism in exchange for ethical purity.

**Lester 12** \[Alan Lester, British historical geographer and author
who has worked for Sussex University since 2000. He was appointed
Professor of Historical Geography in 2006. He is known for his research
on imperial networks, colonial humanitarianism and imperial governance.
\"Humanism, race and the colonial frontier\", Transactions of the
Institute of British Geographers 37 (1), January 2012,
https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/j.1475-5661.2011.00450.x\]//
Micah

As a number of new imperial historians of Britain have stressed, these
scientists effectively strengthened the connections between
settler-originated constructions of race and metropolitan middle-class
constructions of other kinds of 'internal' social difference (Hall
1996; Hall and Rose 2006). In Hunt's view, the brain of the 'Negro' was
much like that of the European woman or child, and in the view of many
contemporaries, both shared certain characteristics with those of
criminals, Celts and the labouring poor, none of whom were
differentiated through their relations with nature in a metropolitan
context (Bolt 1971). Long-held settler representations, such as those
that were reformulated for metropolitan consumption by Knox and Hunt,
became more powerful in Britain because they articulated a language of
inherent difference in which the relations of British class and gender
could now be more conveniently framed. They helped establish, as Ann
Stoler puts it, 'a "mobile" discourse of empire that designated
eligibility for citizenship, class membership, and gendered assignments
to race' (Stoler 1995, 127--8).

Although, as I have argued, both Anderson and I emphasise in practice
those sets of relations on colonial frontiers that gave rise to racial
discursive shifts in the nineteenth century, there are important
postcolonial questions hinging on the distinction between the different
kind of discursive relations that Anderson emphasises, and those that I
have tried to track above.

Anderson argues that

it is not an issue of extending humanity to ... negatively racialised
people, but of putting into question that from which such people have
been excluded -- that which, for liberal discourse, remains
unproblematised. (2007, 199)

I fear, however, that if we direct attention away from histories of
humanism's failure to deal with difference and to render that difference
compatible with its fundamental universalism, and if we overlook its
proponents' failed attempts to combat dispossession, murder and
oppression; if our history of race is instead understood through a
critique of humanity's conceptual separation from nature, we dilute the
political potency of universalism.

[Historically, it was not humanism that gave rise to racial innatism, it
was the specifically anti-humanist politics of settlers forging new
social assemblages through relations of violence on colonial frontiers.
**Settler communities became established social assemblages in their own
right specifically through the rejection of humanist interventions.**
Perhaps, as Edward Said suggested]{.underline}, [we can learn from the
implementation of humanist universalism in practice, and insist on
its *potential* to combat racism, and perhaps we]{.underline} can insist
on the contemporary conceptual hybridisation of human--non-human
entities too[, without necessarily abandoning all the precepts of
humanism]{.underline} (Said 2004; Todorov 2002). [We do not necessarily
need to accord a specific value to the human, separate from and above
nature, in order to make a moral and political case for a fundamental
human universalism that can be wielded strategically against racial
violence.]{.underline}

[Nineteenth century humanitarians' universalism was fundamentally
**conditioned by** their belief that British culture stood at the apex
of a hierarchical order of civilisations. From the mid-nineteenth
century through to the mid-twentieth century, this ethnocentrism
produced what Lyotard describes as 'the flattening of differences, or
the demand for a norm ("human nature")']{.underline}, that 'carries with
it its own forms of terror' (cited Braun 2004, 1352). The intervention
of Aboriginal Protection demonstrates that humanist universalism has the
potential to inflict such terror (it was the Protectorate of Aborigines
Office reincarnated that was responsible, later in the nineteenth and
twentieth centuries, for Aboriginal Australia's Stolen Generation, and
it was the assimilationist vision of the Protectors' equivalents in
Canada that led to the abuses of the Residential Schools system). But we
must not forget that humanism's alternatives, founded upon principles of
difference rather than commonality, have the potential to do the same
and even worse.

In the nineteenth century, Caribbean planters and then emigrant British
settlers emphasised the multiplicity of the human species, the absence
of any universal 'human nature', the incorrigibility of difference, in
their upholding of biological determinism. Their assault on any notion
of a fundamental commonality among human beings has disconcerting points
of intersection with the radical critique of humanism today. The
scientific argument of the nineteenth century that came closest to
post-humanism's insistence on the hybridity of humanity, promising to
'close the ontological gap between human and non-human animals' (Day
2008, 49), was the evolutionary theory of biological descent associated
with Darwin, and yet this theory was adopted in Aotearoa New Zealand and
other colonial sites precisely to legitimate the potential extinction of
other, 'weaker' races in the face of British colonisation on the grounds
of the natural law of a struggle for survival (Stenhouse 1999).

Both the upholding and the rejection of human--nature binaries can thus
result in racially oppressive actions, depending on the contingent
politics of specific social assemblages. Nineteenth century colonial
humanitarians, inspired as they were by an irredeemably ethnocentric and
religiously exclusive form of universalism, at least combatted
exterminatory settler discourses and practices at multiple sites of
empire, and provided spaces on mission and protectorate stations in
which indigenous peoples could be shielded to a very limited extent from
dispossession and murder. They also, unintentionally, reproduced
discourses of a civilising mission and of a universal humanity that
could be deployed by anticolonial nationalists in other sites of empire
that were never invaded to the same extent by settlers, in independence
struggles from the mid-twentieth century. Finally, as Whatmore's
(2002) analysis of the Select Committee on Aborigines reveals, they
provided juridical narratives that are part of the arsenal of weapons
that indigenous peoples can wield in attempts to claim redress and
recompense in a postcolonial world.

[The politics of humanism in practice]{.underline}, [then, was riddled
with contradiction, fraught with particularity and latent with varying
possibilities. It could be *relatively* progressive and liberatory; it
could be dispossessive and culturally genocidal.]{.underline} Within its
repertoire lay potential to combat environmental and biological
determinism and innatism, however, and this should not be forgotten in a
rush to condemn humanism's universalism as well as its anthropocentrism.
It is in the tensions within universalism that the ongoing potential of
an always provisional, self-conscious, flexible and strategic humanism
-- one that now recognises the continuity between the human and the
non-human as well as the power-laden particularities of the male, middle
class, Western human subject -- resides.

In her appraisal of Anderson's work, Nash points to an urgent
contemporary political imperative, concerning the human genome project.
The project provides a resource for strengthening post-humanism's
critique of anthropocentrism, since it has 'confounded ideas of human
genetic distinctiveness and sophistication relative to other animals' by
finding a smaller specifically human genome than expected' (Nash 2008,
465). However, and as in the early nineteenth century, it is not so much
our understanding of human--non-human relations that matters in the
construction of race, but the political uses of the genome project's
findings about variability among humans. As Nash points out,

the narrative of shared human origins 'out of Africa' and accounts of
the genetic unity of humanity are accompanied by a new insistent focus
on the small amount of genetic variation within humanity. The thrust of
this is to supplement the study of the human genome in general with the
study of human genome variation. Most problematically and most
controversially, much of this work is focused on exploring human genetic
variation between those located in the broad conventional categories of
race, or uses racial or ethnic groups in sampling and mapping global
human genetic diversity. So the move to declare the redundancy of race
in light of new knowledge of the human genome has been swiftly followed
by a resurgence of interest in genetic differences between old
categories of race. (2008, 465)

Demarcations between human races thus continue, as a result of the
political entanglements of subordination, inclusion and exclusion,
regardless of evidence about human--nature boundaries. As Saldanha
recognises, given the material, phenotypical basis for ideas of race,
'when understood as an immanent process, it becomes clear that, though
contingent, race cannot be transcended, only understood and rearranged'
(2006, 9). If we are not to use the principle of human universalism,
stripped as far as possible in the light of colonial history of its
colonising prescriptions, to combat persistent innatist demarcations,
however, then what are we to use?

The legacy of humanism may be 'an obstacle, an ideology, a myth'
(Badmington 2004, 1344), and the notion of 'the human' may have to be
redefined contingently and strategically as a moral category rather than
a biological category discrete from the non-human,5 but it may be a
necessary political fiction, providing a resource for the construction
of a new cosmopolitanism (Mignolo 2000), or even, in Fanon's words, a
'new humanism' founded in human immanence rather than transcendence
(Alessandrini 2009; Fanon 1967, 7; Pithouse 2003; Murdoch 2004). We need
a universality of human worth without assuming a universality of either
human biology or culture, and perhaps the best way to achieve this
within a new cosmopolitan humanism is, as Saldanha puts it, not to
'eliminate' race (cf. Gilroy 2000), but to proliferate it -- to direct
its 'many energies''at multiplying racial differences so as to render
them joyfully cacophonic' (Saldanha 2006, 21). The universalism of what
Saldanha calls 'a thousand tiny races' (Saldanha 2006, 21) constituting
humanity may have to exist alongside, and in acknowledged tension with,
the deconstruction of the anthropocentrism that isolates all these races
from 'nature'.

### Theory Wrong\-\--Colonialism\-\--2AC

#### 'Blue colonialism' and its dependence on Orientalism arbitrarily inflate the microhistory of blue pigment and imposes a Eurocentric worldview onto the history of humanism. 

Dorothy **Figueria 8**, Professor of Comparative Literature at the
University of Georgia, Ph.D. in Comparative Studies in Literature from
the University of Chicago, "Postcolonial Criticism and Identitarian
Politics," in *Otherwise Occupied: Pedagogies of Alterity and the
Brahminization of Theory*, pp. 31-48, State University of New York
Press, 2008, accessed 06-27-2025, DG

[Postcolonial criticism invites cross-cultural analysis
without]{.underline} demanding extensive [**cultural knowledge**. It
trades in **stereotypes** and the **fantasy** of locating a genuine
anticolonial nationalistic subject grasped through a psychoanalytic
framework that is itself **not culturally specific**]{.underline}
(Harootunian 2002:172). 7 [This lack of specificity has led]{.underline}
some [critics to compare]{.underline} the discourse of [postcolonial
criticism with]{.underline} that of **[colonial thinking]{.underline}**
(Shohat 1992:99). [Postcolonial theory]{.underline} still [orients the
globe according to a **single binary** of Western
historicism]{.underline} (McClintock 1992:85). [It marks and
markets]{.underline} the multitudinous [cultures]{.underline} of the
world [and telescopes their geopolitical distinctions **into
invisibility**]{.underline} (McClintock 1992:86). [One colonial
experience can come to resemble another]{.underline}. The [critics
**indiscriminately embrace the Other** and level out]{.underline} the
[various competing Others]{.underline}. What is important is that [the
Other **always be perceived as correct, regardless of
differences**]{.underline}. It must fulfill the critic's desire for a
pure otherness in pristine luminosity (Chow 1995:45). In this manner,
[postcolonial criticism]{.underline} exhibits a banal Rousseauism that
[privileges non-Western culture and glories in its presumptive eventual
resurgence as it **topples some establishment**]{.underline} (Clark
1996:44).

The most sustained complaint against postcolonial criticism, however, is
that it [claims **historical method without** a grasp of **historical
facts**]{.underline}. As Aijaz Ahmad (1992) has shown, [if you level out
all histories, you can take]{.underline} up [a thousand available
microhistories **arbitrarily** since they all amount more or less to the
same thing]{.underline}. Relying on the experience of modern
colonialism, [the critic divides history into
**manageable**]{.underline} and [**isolated segments**
while]{.underline} at the same time [arguing against]{.underline} the
false [homogenization]{.underline} of Orientalist projects (Bahri
1995:52). Ahmad bewails the postcolonial theorist's denial of history,
especially the histories of peoples, their trajectories of survival, and
their achievements. For example, the "historical" narrative of Said's
Culture and Imperialism is quite abstract, consisting more of textual
commentary and literary history than history, per se . Bhabha even
vaunts the antihistoricism in his rewriting of the nation as narration.
[By mystifying the political effects of Western postmodern **heg**emony,
frameworks]{.underline} such as Bhabha's cult of linguistic and
psychological ambivalence and Spivak's metaphysics of textualism further
**[obscure]{.underline}** the history of **[peoples' resistance to
imperialism]{.underline}**.

[This lack within postcolonial criticism of **any effective historical
consciousness** explains why it]{.underline} has primarily [found urgent
currency in the first world and few ripples resonate in]{.underline} the
excolonized worlds of [South Asia and Africa. The predominance of
critical contestants in Euroamerican centers reflects how much this
theory is **inherently Eurocentric** and **culture bound**]{.underline}
(Clark 1996:24). Critics of postcolonial theory have begun to question
the agendas lurking behind its rhetoric and its complicity with certain
forms of Eurocentric cultural theory (Radhakrishnan 1993:750) and the
power struggles replicated within this critical discourse. [It
is]{.underline}, indeed, [ironic the degree to which postcolonial theory
depends on European cultural theory while]{.underline}, at the same
time, [claiming]{.underline} that [European knowledge can **never
disclose the truth** regarding the colonized]{.underline} (San Juan
2002:259).

#### It privileges theoretical abstraction over the [particularities]{.underline} of colonial violence.

Swati **Parashar &** Michael **Schulz 21**, Associate Professor of Peace
and Development at the University of Gothenburg, Ph.D. in Politics and
International Relations from Lancaster University; Professor of Peace
and Development at the University of Gothenburg, Ph.D. in Peace and
Development Research from the University of Gothenburg, "Colonial
legacies, postcolonial 'selfhood' and the (un)doing of Africa," Third
World Quarterly, Vol. 42, No. 5, 2021, accessed 06-27-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1080/01436597.2021.1903313, DG

Our point of reflection is that [if colonialism was a violent project,
postcolonial]{.underline} and decolonial [encounters]{.underline} also
[unleash **multiple forms of violence**]{.underline}. We have indicated
that [the Western-style, modern nation-state formula **remains
aspirational** for many counter-hegemonic groups, and for regimes that
want to consolidate power]{.underline} (Parashar 2019). [The process
of]{.underline} '[becoming]{.underline}' has **[unleashed its own
structures of violence]{.underline}**. Bina D'Costa and John Braithwaite
(2018) have shown how both [violent actions and]{.underline} violent
[imaginaries **cascade in**]{.underline} many [**postcolonial
societies**. Crime cascades to war, war]{.underline} cascades [to more
war and to crime, and crime and war both cascade to **state violence**
such as **torture, enforced disappearances** and **extrajudicial
execution**]{.underline}. Gerlach (2010) argues that extremely violent
societies are not violent in some cultural or essential way. Instead,
[societies transition in and out of]{.underline} extremely [violent
periods]{.underline} of their histories as a result of crises.

[Violence is not the defining characteristic of any
society]{.underline}; violence is the result of the socialisation and
exchanges embedded in the very nature of colonial encounters and
subsequently in their various legacies, as we explore here. However,
**[we continue to]{.underline}** erase the histories of people and
societies, especially colonialism and imperialism, to [**justify
violence as a contemporary affliction devoid of context**. In much of
the Global South]{.underline}, former colonies and [now post colonies,
it is not possible to understand]{.underline} contemporary
[violence]{.underline} without its antecedents, [without accounting for
colonial continuities and historical legacies]{.underline}. We do not
mean history here in the ordinary, official, disciplinary sense but
histories as the past that lives and the past with which most people
still live, at least in societies not dominated by historical
consciousness, which is often dependent on shared and private memories,
cultures, myths, legends and epics that bypass the historical mode of
reasoning (Nandy 1995). [Violence is]{.underline} not a product but **[a
process]{.underline}** in these societies, [where the]{.underline}
violent [histories of colonialism are **gradually**]{.underline} being
**[erased]{.underline}** out of the analysis of the contemporary
'political'. What [we get]{.underline} are **[poorly articulated
justifications of colonialism]{.underline}**, often [vile expressions of
white fragility and emasculation that masquerade as
informed]{.underline} '[viewpoints]{.underline}' [and
research]{.underline} about a textured and multi-layered past.

The erasure of violence on the Global South was attended to in one of
the recent special issues in Third World Quarterly on 'Violence and
Ordering of the Third World' (Persaud and Kumarakulasingam 2019). The
editors made a powerful case to centre the violence (especially body
counts) that has been a definitive and structurally constitutive factor
in the contact between the West and the Third World, through
colonisation and occupation, and through mimicry practices of
postcolonial states invested in Western modernity and the nation-state
system. States had to be willed into homogeneous, masculine,
geographical entities, denying difference and diversity. As the editors,
Persaud and Kumarakulsingam, postulated in their introduction,

> [Violence in]{.underline} such [colonial contexts has taken multiple
> forms, ranging from]{.underline} the [everyday rituals]{.underline} of
> extracting submission for labour exploitation, [to outright, total
> war]{.underline}. These regimes of violence include but are not
> limited to everyday disciplinary punishment to maintain 'order' (espe-
> cially in slavery and indentureship), massacres, saturation bombing of
> peoples and landscapes, genocide and near extermination. (2019, 199)

The [silences around these issues were discussed]{.underline} by the
various authors [as an act of **active forgetting** that **privileged
theoretical abstraction**]{.underline}. We take these conversations
further, revisiting the impact of colonialism's violence on postcolonial
politics and development in Africa. The discussions in this volume draw
insights from postcolonial developments and discourses in Cameroon,
Ethiopia, Ghana, Kenya, Liberia, Mozambique, Nigeria, Rwanda, Sierra
Leone, South Africa, South Sudan, Togo and Uganda.

### Theory Wrong\-\--Coloniality in IR\-\--2AC

#### 'Coloniality' is neither unifying nor should it serve as a catch-all concept for explaining global oppression.

Doerthe **Rosenow 24**, Lecturer in International Relations at King's
College London, Ph.D. in War Studies from King's College London, "The
Violence of Settler Imperialism -- and Why the Concept of Coloniality
Cannot Grasp It," Millenium: Journal of International Studies, Vol. 52,
No. 2, February 2024, accessed 06-27-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1177/03058298231202554, DG

The concept of 'coloniality' seems to offer the right tool for such an
all-encompassing understanding and analysis, and it might be for that
reason that it is now increasingly used in the social sciences. Similar
to what is going on in the public realm, [academia]{.underline} also
[attempts to come to terms with colonial legacies]{.underline}. Ánibal
Quijano uses the term 'coloniality' to describe the continuities of
global colonial rationalities and practices. 'Coloniality' names a
system of capitalist-colonial expansion that started in the 15th century
with the conquering of the Americas, relying on the creation of an
exploitative global division of labour. This division of labour was
based on and justified through the establishment of a sociocultural,
racialised hierarchy, and went along with the suppression of any
non-capitalist/non-European ways to know and live, on the basis of the
project of 'modernity' at large. 'Coloniality', according to Quijano,
has not ended, as formal colonialism did -- it brought the world as we
know it into being, and continues to hold it in its thrall.3

[Because coloniality makes sense of past and ongoing oppression in
global terms]{.underline}, it seems a particularly apt concept for
International Relations (IR), which, after all, is interested in
understanding the nature of global power. It might be for this reason
that [there is]{.underline} now [an ever-expanding body of IR literature
that uses the term]{.underline}, ranging from simply using coloniality
as a non-distinct descriptor of global colonial relations, to being used
in place of or in addition to terms such as 'race', 'colonialism',
'raciality', 'slavery' or 'Eurocentrism', to those scholars who situate
themselves strongly in the Latin American decolonial tradition
(sometimes even explicitly identifying themselves as 'decolonial'
scholars).4

However, even when coloniality is used in a manner that demonstrates a
strong understanding of its intellectual legacy, [its suitability as
a]{.underline} '[catch-all]{.underline}' [concept]{.underline} that can
describe our global condition at large [is **rarely
interrogated**]{.underline}. As Karen Tucker argues, [there is a danger
that coloniality becomes a way to describe]{.underline} '[systems of
oppression and domination]{.underline}' [as]{.underline}
'**[uniform]{.underline}**' [and]{.underline} '[unitary]{.underline}',
[at the expense of]{.underline} conducting '[**close, detailed
analysis** of the dispersed practices that produce racialized
hierarchies and erasures]{.underline}'.5 In this article, I aim to
demonstrate that [this tendency to describe colonial oppression as
unitary and homogeneous is **inherent in the** way that the **concept**
was designed]{.underline} by Quijano, [and]{.underline} then further
[consolidated by]{.underline} other [decolonial scholars who became key
reference points in IR]{.underline}. As I will show, [this has
problematic consequences, **not only for understanding**]{.underline}
the nature of [**colonial oppression** but **also for the resistance
against it**. Coloniality is grounded in]{.underline} particular
[assumptions that not only **render certain colonial violence
invisible** but are even in danger of **reinforcing that
violence**]{.underline}.

### Theory Wrong\-\--Blue Humanities\-\--2AC

#### 'Blue humanities' get co-opted by the military and can't escape colonial knowledge production. 

Bianca Louise **Hennessy 21**, Ph.D. candidate in Pacific Studies at the
Australian National University, "The Possibilities of Decolonial Pacific
Studies: Learning from an Oceanic Genealogy of Transformative Academic
Practice," The Australian National University, 2021, accessed
06-28-2025, ProQuest, DG

There is another reason to be careful about specificity: these
[capitalist and militarist interventions]{.underline} in the Pacific
[tend to insidiously **co-opt terminology** and **framings of
expansiveness and fluidity**]{.underline}. Elizabeth DeLoughrey's essay
'Critical Ocean Studies for the Anthropocence' makes this point. In an
argument that cautions those associated with [the]{.underline} 'oceanic
or [blue humanities]{.underline}' -- to which I would add Pacific
studies -- she notes that,

> Fluidity, mobility, adaptability, and flux -- all terms associated
> with neoliberal globalization regimes as well the oceanic or blue
> humanities -- are also key words and strategies of
> twenty-first-century maritime militarism. (DeLoughrey 2019, 25)

In explaining the expansiveness of maritime militarism, DeLoughrey
examines RIMPAC (Rim of the Pacific Exercise), a biennial military
exercise conducted by US-allied Pacific Rim nations (Figure 3.9). Though
its headquarters are in US military sites in Hawai‛i, RIMPAC's naval and
air-based weaponry stretch, in an arguably absurdly vast scale, across
the Pacific. DeLoughrey cites a US federal court ruling in 2015 which
noted the 'breathtaking assertion' that the US Navy assumed its right to
access literally all of the Pacific, unlimited by 'time, space, species,
or depth' (DeLoughrey 2019, 31). Its claim to use such vast space to
perform military might is underscored by the fact that the 'US
Indo--Pacific Command,' based in Hawai‛i, claims responsibility for
US-led military operations spanning 100 million square miles (260
million square kilometres), a staggering 'fifty-two percent of the
Earth's surface' (DeLoughrey 2019, 24). The United States military, the
progenitor of colonial occupation of Pacific Islander lands and seas
throughout much of the north Pacific, clearly demonstrates an almost
endless quest for terrestrial and aquatic expansion -- much like the
ever-expanding, land-seeking logics that underscore settler colonialism
(Veracini 2011, 3). A sea of islands, vast and hospitable in the 'Our
Sea of Islands' sense, becomes twisted insidiously to serve militarist
ends (see also Allen et al. 2018).

Indeed, the [conceptual framings]{.underline} of area studies [have
expanded and contracted **precisely** in line with how hegemonic nations
such as the **U**nited **S**tates have perceived their real and desired
spheres of influence]{.underline}. Nowhere is this more relevant than
the Pacific, where capitalism and the arrangement of global alliances
have deigned the liquid zone of the great ocean as one where
'co-prosperity' of rim nations is predicated upon the movement of
capital and ideas through the Pacific, not of the Pacific, considering
it a moving marketplace rather than anything peopled or historied
(Connery 1994; Jolly 2007, 525--26).4 [These arguments remind us to **be
cautious of unquestioned fluidity**, and as who it empowers and who, or
what, it exploits. The threat of co-optation **forever
looms**]{.underline}, as it does with decolonisation itself (Tuck and
Yang 2012).

Pacific studies' decolonial mission of restoring rhetoric,
representative and real power to Pacific Islanders must be wary of such
co-optation. [If notions of place invoke ways of thinking]{.underline}
in Pacific studies, [we tend to celebrate expansive and mobile
approaches to scholarly work through metaphors of the
ocean]{.underline}. For example, in Geoffrey White's introduction to the
anthology of Hau‛ofa's works, he recognises the use of the ocean as 'a
trope for perpetual motion and freedom as well as vastness and depth,'
that Hau‛ofa's Pacific is 'startlingly expansive and inclusive,' and
that the ocean provides 'metaphors that allow for openness and
connection' (G. White 2008). [Such tropes and metaphors are certainly
useful in resisting the constraints placed on bodies and ideas by
enduring settler colonial and neoliberal nation-states]{.underline}, but
[we do a **disservice to those people and knowledges by not also
attending to their particular ways of being, knowing and doing, and we
need specificity of mobility**]{.underline} (like Hau‛ofa's anecdote
about his Tongan friend flying across the Pacific) **[to ward
off]{.underline}** pervasive **[co-opting threats of territory-seeking
capitalism, colonialism and militarism]{.underline}**. In Diaz's keynote
on Milanesian seafaring, he reminds us that the title of 'Our Sea of
Islands' features a sea, not an ocean -- a liminal zone linked to
landmasses, where fluid potentiality meets anchored and rooted edges of
shoreline (see also Kauvaka 2016). He writes that '[some of us, and our
seas, consistently get **washed out by the rush to expansive ideas of
oceanic fluidity**]{.underline}' (Diaz 2018, 25) -- **[an erasure that
does not serve Pacific Islanders wherever they may lay down roots, or
indeed, routes]{.underline}**.

#### Blue humanities as a scholarly body is [insulated from criticism]{.underline} and fails to properly engage with its dissidents. 

Ian **Buchanan 21**, Professor of Cultural Studies at the University of
Wollongong, Ph.D. in English and Comparative Literature from Murdoch
University, "The Unsustainability of Sustainability," Symploke, Vol. 29,
No 1/2, 2021, accessed 06-28-2025, ProQuest, DG

I will start by saying what I welcomed in Sustaining Seas because the
bulk of this review will be taken up with an analysis of what I found to
be unwelcome. To me, the most important aspect of this collection is the
way it offers an exciting illustration of what a
hands-reaching-across-the-table collaboration between the humanities and
the sciences can look like. The \"Operation Crayweed\" project led by
Australian marine ecologist Adriana Vergės is a model in this regard.
\"If there is a message from major environmental issues such as climate
change it is that scientific evidence in and of itself does not convince
the broader public of the need for action or even caring about the
environment\" (242-43). Several chapters in this collection echo this
point, which is welcome indeed, and for the most part they suggest that
the sciences need to work with artists to produce works of art, stories,
songs and other varieties of art work to engage the \"broader public,\"
about which I can only say that would surely be welcome. However, I
would suggest that [the humanities can do more than make
art]{.underline}, as valuable as that is; [it]{.underline} can also, I
would say [must]{.underline}, **[engage in]{.underline}** what used to
be known as ideologiekritik, which is the process of **[rigorously
examining and]{.underline}** where necessary **[critiquing the concepts,
assumptions, and ideological positions that underpin a given piece of
scholarly work]{.underline}**. We need to unite not just the creative
and the scientific, but also the scientific and the critical, [if we are
to develop]{.underline} the nascent field of the \"[blue
humanities]{.underline}\" [into a genuine field of scholarly
inquiry]{.underline}. I might as well be blunt here and say it is the
critical element that I find lacking in this collection.

It is difficult to respond to any edited collection, particularly one as
richly interdisciplinary as Sustaining Seas, without risking turning it
into a grading exercise in which you single out the papers you like and
the ones you don\'t, or worse, in typical Žižekian fashion, stipulating
which papers are right and which ones are wrong. In the hope of avoiding
that fate, I will instead treat it as multifaceted group project, or if
that still sounds too much like a grading exercise, then as a
collaborative mission. Although the collection as a whole is internally
heterogeneous, particularly with regards to disciplinary background and
disciplinary ways of writing (humanities and social sciences really are
different!), it is remarkably homogeneous with regards the varieties of
modalities of relating to marine life it espouses. This might be
expected of a collection that originated with a conference, so it may
just be an artifact of the self-selecting nature of conferences rather
than a conscious editorial choice. [The absence of debate]{.underline}
in these pages [is noteworthy because many of the positions it adopts
are **problematic**]{.underline}, as I will try to show in what follows;
[it]{.underline} also [seems]{.underline} to be symptomatic of a
systematic [avoidance of the kinds of discourses]{.underline}-Marxism in
particular-[that could not but disrupt the apparent consensus this set
of authors has settled into]{.underline}. One cannot stay with the
trouble as Haraway suggests is necessary in these troubling times if one
is not also prepared to make trouble as I aim to do here.

To my mind, [the most **glaring** and indeed troubling
**absence**]{.underline} in this whole collection **[is any discussion
of capitalism]{.underline}** as a world-system. One might respond by
saying: well you can\'t include everything, not even in a capacious
volume like this. To which I would reply: it is the one thing that
cannot not be included. As Jennifer Telesca writes in her recent book
Red Gold on the demise of the bluefin tuna, it is capitalism as a
world-system, not the practices of fishers, which drives the \"predatory
regime of value\" that is pushing all sea life towards extinction
(Teslsca 2020, 3). As such, [no discussion of the present state of
affairs can legitimately sidestep it]{.underline} as this collection
consistently tries to do [because to do so is to **mistake symptoms for
causes**]{.underline}. I say sidestep because capitalism\'s effects are
frequently acknowledged here but capitalism itself as the motor cause of
history is passed over in silence. [An accusing finger is occasionally
pointed at]{.underline} \"[neoliberalism]{.underline},\" [but it
is]{.underline} largely [treated as though it referred to a lifestyle
choice rather than a way of organising society]{.underline} around a
specific structure of value that is inherently toxic to all life on
earth. In what amounts to a bait and switch move, [this collection
condemns largescale industrial fishing for hoovering up vast quantities
of sea life, only to then turn around and say that things would be fine
if we returned to the small-scale fishing practices of traditional and
indigenous peoples. In this way it **avoids condemning the
system**]{.underline}-i.e., the predatory regime of value of
capitalism-that underpins both large and small-scale forms of fishing.

Old school Marxists would not hesitate to call this way of viewing
things false consciousness and lest this charge seem too harsh let me
run the highlighter pen over a couple of different iterations of it
because it is the plasticity of this idea that is the surest sign of its
ideological function. In the chapter pointedly titled, \"Ocean Regime
Shift,\" South African scholar Lesley Green writes \"Regime-changing
ocean management sciences will not render marine creatures as
extractables but will prioritize the desires of creatures, including
humans, to survive and live well\" (Probyn, Johnston, and Lee 2020, 24).
This argument is consistent with Telecsa\'s position inasmuch as it
challenges the idea that marine life should be regarded as resources to
be profited from, but then Green goes onto say this \"ocean regime
shift\" requires that priority be given to \"the forms of care
articulated by small-scale fishers in the drafting of the small-scale
fisher\'s policy, to overcome the destructive \'thingification\' that
undergirds the conditions leading to an Anthropocene\" (24). It is not
made clear, though, how reorienting fishing policy around the needs and
interests of small-scale fishers would cause us to cease thinking of
fish as \"extractables\" and \"things,\" not least because it encourages
us to continue to think of fish as \"extractables\" and \"things\" for
humans to consume and profit from, providing only that they do so on a
small-scale.

There is a similar tension in Elspeth Probyn\'s chapter \"\'The Sea is
Empty\': Fishers, Migrants, and a Watery Humanism,\" which extends
points made in her monograph Eating the Ocean (2016) that (as the title
indicates) is predicated on the idea that humans can and should eat
fish. Noting that the ocean\'s inhabitants have a \"bleak present and
even bleaker future,\" Probyn nevertheless insists that the charge of
\"overexploitation\" of marine life-a state of being that is nowhere
challenged in this collection-needs to be \"differentiated\" because it
is \"an abstraction that masks the vast differences between small-scale
and the industrial trawlers that comb the seas, often engaged in the
illegal, unregulated, and unreported theft of fish\" (29). As with the
Green example above[, it is not clear what critical purpose]{.underline}
this particular [differentiation would serve because overexploitation is
overexploitation regardless of who is doing the
over-exploiting]{.underline}. We may speculate that if industrial scale
fishing had not emerged in the 1950s that things might be different
today, but we have no way of knowing if that really is the case and the
simple fact is that a fleet of small boats owned by multiple small-scale
fishers is just as capable of depleting the ocean of its living
creatures as large trawlers are, as indeed are indigenous fishers,
albeit at a slower pace, if they are motivated to do so by a growing and
increasingly profitable market.

No mention is made either of the fact that the global population has
almost doubled since the beginning of industrial fishing in the 1950s
making it very unlikely indeed that the old ways would be any better (in
the old days, too, bluefin tuna was sold as cat food, fertilizer, or
just buried as worthless, which is hardly a model of sustainability). In
other words, it is not \"a dumbing down of caring for the ocean\" to
point out that regardless of the \"complexity of relations within and of
the more-than-human marine\" (28), fish stocks are profoundly and
perilously low, as Probyn acknowledges. Overexploitation is not in this
sense an abstraction; it is rather a generalized empirical state of
being-marine life literally has been overexploited by commercial fishers
everywhere - as such we ought to embrace the term as a necessary
starting point.

The \"[real abstraction]{.underline}\" (to borrow Patel and Moore\'s
useful formulation) [is to be found in the implied]{.underline}
\"[lost]{.underline}\" [positive, namely the thoroughly abstract notion
of the]{.underline} \"[right amount]{.underline}\" [of exploitation
which by some mystical process of calculation we should be able to
arrive at]{.underline} (Patel and Moore 2018, 47). Regulatory bodies,
such as the International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic
Tunas (ICCAT), which Telesca studies, refer to this as the Maximum
Sustainable Yield (MSY) (Telesca 2020, 48-53). The best that can be said
of MSY as a way of thinking about marine life is that despite its highly
complex calculations it nevertheless failed to achieve the one thing it
was supposed to do, namely prevent fish stocks from falling into
decline. This collection dances around this issue, without ever really
finding a landing place that is more concrete than the Orwellian
formulation that largescale fishers are bad and small-scale fishers are
good, which is nothing more than a romanticisation of something that
never existed (humans have always taken as much as they can, it\'s just
that now the capacity to take has reached the species ending level).
Where it is addressed it is generally treated as an issue that can and
should be resolved by policy.

The one paper that addresses regulatory frameworks directly, \"Out of
Sight, Out of Mind: The Challenge of Regulating High Seas Fisheries\" by
Rosemary Rayfuse, gives us little or no cause to think that this is a
viable position. Reviewing the 1982 United Nations Law of the Sea
convention, which determines which nations control which parts of the
world\'s oceans, essentially dividing it between coastal zones
controlled by countries whose coast it is and the high seas which are
designated as commons, Rayfuse writes that we now know all the
assumptions underpinning the construction of this document were
\"ill-conceived,\" to which she adds it is now \"a matter of historical
fact that.. .coastal states have by and large failed to properly manage
their fishery resources, resulting in overexploitation\" (Probyn,
Johnston, and Lee 2020, 144-145). Here again, though, no mention is made
of the system driving overexploitation, namely capitalism.

This in turn points us toward another abstraction that really does stand
in need of some fine-grained differentiation: the concept of
sustainability. Surprisingly for a collection with the title Sustaining
Seas, [there is no discussion whatsoever of the problematic nature of
the concept of sustainability]{.underline}, by which I mean no one
questions whether or not the idea is even tenable. I imagine this is
because to do so would be to question whether humans have any business
eating seafood at all. The unstated premise of sustainability is that
humans are \"natural\" predators of the sea with a \"natural\" right to
eat sea creatures and that this can be done in a balanced way (i.e.,
along the same lines that we imagine is true of the relation between
sharks and other fish). For Probyn, whose research grant funded the
conference, human lives and the lives of marine animals are
\"entangled,\" which she defines as meaning something like symbiotic,
with the corresponding implication that humans have a \"natural\" right
to eat marine life because they have always done so. As I have argued
elsewhere, this position is conceptually flawed because while it is
manifestly true that humans cannot live without marine life, not least
because it supplies 70% of the oxygen we breathe, it is doubtful indeed
that marine life would suffer all that much were humans to disappear. As
such, insisting on the entanglement of humans and marine life is really
just a way of legitimating the ethically questionable (to say the least)
position that humans can and should consume marine life to support its
protein needs.

Sustainability is generally treated in this collection as an ideal that
is within reach, if only we could get the governance right. But as this
collection amply documents, to date the governance has been a long way
from right, where right would mean the fair, equitable and sustainable
distribution of a biological resource and not the absolute protection of
vanishing species, which is another category altogether. There are
several key policy disconnects that have been identified. For example,
while Australia rigorously polices catches in its own waters, imported
catches are regulated as food so its sustainability is never questioned
(75). Fish caught in the so-called \"high seas,\" i.e., outside the
jurisdiction of individual nations, are not subject to any enforceable
controls beyond the handshakes and gentleman\'s agreements (as Telesca
calls them) of international NGOs such as ICCAT (143). The governance
systems have tended to be created with large-scale fishers in mind, and
as such they are not fit for purpose for dealing with small-scale
fishers, tending to place upon them an insupportable compliance burden
(185). [The faith that is placed in policy frameworks]{.underline} here
[despite the mounting evidence of policy failures is]{.underline} quite
**[remarkable]{.underline}**, though perfectly consistent with the
findings of policy ethnographers like Tess Lea who show [that is the
default position of]{.underline} almost all policy workers and, lately
of [cultural studies]{.underline}. Indeed, not to think this way is to
be accused of cynicism.2 [It would]{.underline} also [induce a **crisis
of faith** for cultural studies as a field]{.underline}-[if as cultural
studies practitioners we cannot assume that our knowledge-producing work
is contributing to world-changing policy mechanisms, then what in fact
can we believe in?]{.underline} But [such a crisis of faith
is]{.underline} also perhaps **[overdue]{.underline}** because
**[putting one\'s faith in policy in the way cultural studies sometimes
does is to forget]{.underline}**-or least to not sufficiently attend
to-[**the fact that policy is an instrument of power**. To see it as a
perfectible tool for improving the world]{.underline}, as cultural
studies is wont to do, [is not simply naive, it is a category mistake
that **confuses symptom**]{.underline} (**[policy]{.underline}**) **[and
cause]{.underline}** (**[power]{.underline}**).

[Cultural studies has been actively engaged in]{.underline} what might
usefully and justly be termed [a **governmentality
project**]{.underline} (i.e., it has sought to contribute to and further
the aims of governmentality) for the past three decades. The early
advocates of this turn in cultural studies about which Jameson has been
very sharp in his critique, were indeed inspired by Foucault\'s work on
governmentality, but apparently drew the opposite conclusion to Foucault
as to how one should think about policy discourse. Far from freeing
itself from the category as \"subjugated knowledge,\" as Foucault puts
it, [cultural studies has plunged itself deeper into the abyss by
becoming the **willing servant of** the very
thing]{.underline}-**[governmentality]{.underline}**-[it set out to
investigate and it has]{.underline} thereby [contributed to the
**masking of power** with its elaborations of]{.underline} \"[functional
coherences]{.underline}\" [and]{.underline} \"[formal
systematizations]{.underline}\" [of policy frameworks]{.underline}
(Foucault 2003, 7). One cannot but agree here with Jameson\'s appraisal
of [cultural studies use of concepts like]{.underline}
\"[power]{.underline},\" but one could easily add \"policy\" to the
list, it [is]{.underline}, he said, [a]{.underline} \"**[dangerous and
intoxicating slogan for intellectuals, who]{.underline}** thereby
**[feel]{.underline}** themselves **[closer to its \'reality\' than they
may actually be\"]{.underline}** (Jameson 2008, 627). One must ask, what
has the field really gained by hitching its wagon to policy in this way,
as enabler rather than critic? This is obviously not the place to
attempt such an evaluation, but I will say that any such evaluation will
have to be prepared to find that the field got less than the going rate
of thirty pieces of silver for its efforts because it is evident that in
many cases its faith in the policy machine was misplaced.

Telesca\'s work is sobering in this respect because as she demonstrates
in the case of bluefin tuna, the species management policy achieved
precisely what it set out to achieve: the distribution of a biological
asset and the growth of a global industry worth hundreds of millions of
dollars. The coming extinction of the bluefin tuna was not in this sense
a policy failure: the preservation and protection of the bluefin tuna
was never ICCAT\'s actual goal. To even approach ICCAT in terms of its
policy successes and failures is to put oneself inside the belly of the
policy beast. In a striking passage that sums up everything that-in my
view-is missing from Sustaining Seas, Telesca writes:

In the long run, we need to know how common publics, state bureaucrats,
marine scientists, industrial fishers, environmentalists, and a host of
other actors complicit in ICCAT\'s regulatory action are drawn into -
and consent to - the way fisheries management organized by the
nation-state has administered extermination over time for commodity
empires. We must have an account of why people acquiesce to ICCAT\'s
exterminatory regime of value. (Telesca 2020, 39)

Lastly, [one might have imagined that using a word like care to describe
the]{.underline} \"[ideal]{.underline}\" [relation between humans and
marine animals would preclude the former from consuming the latter, but
in fact as this collection reiterates]{.underline} in several places
[the exact opposite]{.underline} is seen to be true: eating marine life
is caring for marine life. Thus, [one must wonder what exactly this new
ocean regime]{.underline} Green calls for [will really mean for the
ocean\'s inhabitants]{.underline}, other than to be \"harvested\" for
profit and protein by small-scale fishers? In what sense is that to be
rendered either non-extractable or de-thingified?

### Theory Wrong\-\--Blue Humanities\-\--Canals

#### It can't account for [canals]{.underline}. 

Jimmy **Packham 24**, Associate Professor of North American Literature
at the University of Birmingham, Ph.D. from the University of Bristol,

https://doi.org/10.1080/0047729X.2024.2428454, DG

[Oceans roar, streams murmur, brooks babble]{.underline}. But [what

[telling]{.underline}. To establish a place for the canal within the
blue humanities is to be struck initially by the sense that [the canal
**does not exist and**]{.underline} does not **[operate in ways
analogous to other familiar water bodies]{.underline}**. This might also
go some way to explaining the general silence surrounding canals in the
blue humanities. [The canal is not marked by the same alluring qualities
as other major bodies of water: it is not obviously defined by dramatic

appearances. [Nor are you ever really in the canal]{.underline}
(hopefully), [in contrast to seas and rivers, in whose waters one might

like rivers or streams. Taken holistically, [it is precisely because of

### Theory Wrong\-\--Ballot K\-\--2AC

#### The alternative cannot solve it so long as it requires the judge's affirmation. 

Ilan **Kapoor 24**, Professor of Critical Development Studies at the

invested can people become in identity]{.underline} (their own and that

### Theory Wrong\-\--Settler Colonialism\-\--2AC

#### Describing settler colonialism as ontological naturalizes the erasure of Indigenous people. 

Callum **Stewart &** Franka **Vaughan 24**, Ph.D. candidate in the
School of Social and Political Sciences at the University of Melbourne,
M.Phil. in Sociology from the University of Cambridge; Postdoctoral
Researcher and Lecturer in Development Studies at the University of
Melbourne, Ph.D. in Political Science from the University of Melbourne,
"Race, sovereignty, and White settler colonial studies: placing settlers
at the Cultural Interface," Settler Colonial Studies, Latest Articles,
10-02-2024, accessed 06-27-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1080/2201473X.2024.2408145, DG

[White settler colonial studies has been]{.underline} widely [criticised
for producing a sense of]{.underline} '**[colonial
fatalism]{.underline}**', for [representing settler colonialism
as]{.underline} '**[inevitable]{.underline}**', [and]{.underline} for
[reproducing a]{.underline} '**[teleology of elimination]{.underline}**'
**[of Indigenous lifeworlds]{.underline}**.77 In this section, we
therefore turn to theorise the background against which White settler
colonial studies is produced as a discipline. Wolfe contends that, in
settler colonial contexts, race functions primarily as an 'organizing
grammar' or 'ideology' that may 'obscure the primary historical
relation- ship of invasion'.78 We argue, however, that [White settler
colonial studies takes]{.underline} the [macrological dynamics and
institutionalised frameworks]{.underline} of race [as the **self-evident
background of the settler subject**]{.underline}. White settler colonial
studies is [conditioned by histories of European imperialism and racial
capitalism that **orient White settler standpoints towards White
sovereignty**]{.underline}. From a White settler standpoint, [the
discipline **produces the settler as transhistorical**, and **forecloses
possibilities for action that emerge from Indigenous
sovereignty**]{.underline}.

Wolfe's description of the settler/Indigenous binary is preconditioned
by the naturalised racial difference of British imperialism. Wolfe
argues that the frontier presents the 'primary paradigm' of 'the
historical development of Australian settler-colonisation'.79 The
frontier, for Wolfe, is a performative representation that produces a
primary settler/ Indigenous binary. According to Wolfe, the 'binary
nature of the Native/settler divide is a structural reality of a
zero-sum conflict between two polarised and mutually distinct
parties'.80 In his early work, Wolfe argues that 'retrospectively,
polarity is indisputable -- back from a certain point, there is no
question as to the mutual distinctiveness of the two parties to the
Australian colonial process. The differences within them were smaller
than the differences between them'.81 In response to critics, Wolfe
forcefully repeats this point later in his career:

> the concept of the frontier has the virtue of expressing the protean
> fact of a historical coming together of societies that had previously
> been mutually discrete ... Go back far enough, in other words, and
> there can be no disputing the existence of an unqualified empirical
> binarism.82

Wolfe's [analysis]{.underline} of Australia thereby [assumes the
existence of two pre-given and categorically different entities that
precede the invasion]{.underline}.

[Wolfe theoretically argues]{.underline} that [settler/Indigenous
polarity is]{.underline} '[a **structural relationship rather than an
effect of the will**]{.underline}'.83 As such, he [categorises all
non-Indigenous peoples living on stolen Indigenous land as
settlers]{.underline}. This risks [**overdetermining the position of
non-Indigenous peoples on stolen Indigenous land**, producing a
**settler homogeneity** by locating Black enslaved peoples, forced
migrants of colour, and Indigenous migrants **into the White category of
settler**]{.underline}. Wolfe provides a number of counter-intuitive
examples of settlers. Most contentiously, Wolfe argues that:

> The fact that enslaved peoples immigrated against their will ... does
> not alter the structural fact that their presence, however
> involuntary, was part of the process of Native dispossession.84

Similarly, Wolfe cites the recruitment of Native Peruvians to bolster
the population of Israeli colonies in occupied Palestine, and the
deployment of Native American soldiers in Hawaii as evidence of the
'determinate nature of structural relations' irrespective of the
conditions that motivated their migration.85 British settlers
nevertheless enter Wolfe's analysis as the implicit point of reference
from which the category of settler is theorised. Wolfe justifies his
counter-intuitive stance by comparing enslaved peoples in America and
Indigenous-cum-settlers in Israel and Hawai'i to White settlers in
Australia: 'White convicts came against their will. Does this mean that
their descendants are not settlers?'.86 Wolfe's settler/Indigenous
binary might therefore be rearticulated as a White/Indigenous-race
binary; a White/Black binary in the case of Australia.

Wolfe's [description of the logic of elimination is preconditioned by
naturalised bioeconomic laws of racial capitalism]{.underline}. Wolfe's
[geographical depiction of the frontier]{.underline}, as noted above,
[presents Indigenous-settler relations in terms of a]{.underline}
'**[zero-sum conflict]{.underline}**' over the land.87
[This]{.underline} description of a zero-sum conflict [presents the land
as a finite and scarce resource, **a possession to be secured through**
the **exclusion** of others]{.underline}. Wolfe's description of the
logic of elimination produces a slippage in his work 'between the intent
of settler colonialism and its tangible outcomes, which carry the
implication of extinction'.88 Jean M. O'Brien identifies two examples of
this slippage in Wolfe's Traces of History. First, Wolfe describes
allotment policy in the USA, which aimed to eliminate Native Americans
from the land by requiring that they either move further west or
assimilate into the settler nation by accepting a privatised allotment
of land. Wolfe, however, concludes that those Choctaw people who opted
to stay on their land and take up individual allotments were
successfully transformed into '"homesteaders and American citizens"';
for Wolfe, 'Without the tribe ... for all practical purposes, they were
no longer Indians'.89 Second, Wolfe describes the Indian Reorganization
Act (IRA) of 1934 as a policy that aimed to eliminate Native Americans
by reforming and assimilating them into the settler nation at the level
of the tribe. Wolfe again concludes that those 'reformed tribe\[s\] had
been domesticated' through the reservation system and the 'inassimilable
features of the tribe' eliminated.90 He further concludes that Native
Americans off-reservation were thereby incorporated into the settler
nation as either Black or White, and 'ceased being Indian'.91 In each
example, [Wolfe's account of the logic of elimination slips into a
description of the]{.underline} '[social death of
Nativeness]{.underline}'92 [as an actual outcome of settler colonialism.
By theorising settler colonialism as bioeconomic competition over land,
Wolfe **naturalises the elimination of the Indigenous race** **and their
replacement by the White race**]{.underline}.

Finally, Veracini's description of settler sovereignty is preconditioned
by White possessive logics of both racial capitalism and British
imperialism. Veracini describes settlers as 'founders of political
orders', and a 'sovereign collective';93 a settler body politic is
'self-constituted ... through a foundational sovereign movement across
space'.94 Settlers, for Veracini, 'carry their sovereignty with them'.95
Veracini's [description of sovereignty as something settlers may carry
across space represents settler sovereignty as a White
possession]{.underline}. He details, for example, the corporate
foundations of an 'extensive' list of settler colonial endeavours.96 In
these endeavours, on Veracini's account, the sovereignty of settlers is
a product of the economic capital they carry with them. 'Capitalism',
for Veracini, 'is at the service of settlement'. In another example,
Veracini argues [that all settler colonial endeavours must
have]{.underline} '[the ability to will a collective identity and its
institutions into existence]{.underline}'.97 In other words, settler
bodies can only irrupt into existence if they are able to imagine their
movement to a new location. This [reflects]{.underline}, then, [a public
and psychological wage of British imperialism. Veracini produces
Whiteness as a possession through the construction of migrants
as]{.underline} '[appellants **facing a political order that is already
constituted'**]{.underline}.98 [This]{.underline} construction [risks
**dehumanising migrants** by **erasing their agency** and **presenting
them as chattel or imperial subjects who may be easily**]{.underline}
'**[co-opted within settler colonial political
regimes]{.underline}**'.99 [Racial capitalism and British
imperialism]{.underline} thus [function as the background to settlers'
apparent capacity to carry their sovereignty with them wherever they
go]{.underline}. As such, Veracini concludes that 'sovereign entitlement
... travels with the settler collective'. Whiteness as possession
provides settlers with what Veracini terms a 'crucial marker of settler
substantive sovereignty', the ability of settlers to biopolitically
manage the 'population economy' of their territory.100 Here, Veracini's
[description of settler sovereignty as **substantive, rather than
imagined**]{.underline}, further **[represents Indigenous peoples as if
they are already captured and contained by settler sovereignty. It
reproduces a White fantasy of]{.underline}** '**[ownership of the earth,
forever and ever, Amen!]{.underline}**'

Conclusion

This article has sought to theorise the racialisation of settler
subjects and their practices by locating settlers within global and
transnational histories of European and US imperialism, racial
capitalism, and settler colonialism. It departs from White settler
colonial studies' description of settler colonialism as a 'structure,
not an event', instead using Nakata's Cultural Interface theory to
unsettle settlers' place on sovereign Indigenous land. For Nakata,
Indigenous sovereignty is the starting point of the analysis, not the
object of analysis. Through the analysis of White sovereignty and
settler orientations, we extend Cultural Interface theory to theorise
how [the transhistorical appearance of the settler within White settler
colonial studies is **conditioned by race**]{.underline}. To do so, we
have explored the shape of the White settler standpoints taken up by
both White colonisers and Black American settlers in Liberia, and in
White settler colonial studies itself. We argue that [White settler
colonial studies reproduces the orientation of White settler standpoints
towards White sovereignty, **rendering possibilities that emerge from
Indigenous sovereignty unintelligible**]{.underline}.

Through the analysis of White sovereignty and settler orientations, we
have extended Nakata's work to theorise the conditions that produce
settlers at the Cultural Interface. Situating Du Bois and
Moreton-Robinson in conversation, we have highlighted the theoretical
connections that the analytic of White possessive sovereignty
illuminates. From racial capitalism and chattel slavery, to British
imperialism and settler colonial nations, Du Bois and Moreton-Robinson
theorise Whiteness as a possession performatively accumulated through
the dehumanisation and dispossession of others, and which rationalises
White sovereign ownership of the earth and everything in it. Through the
example of Liberia, we have sought to further map White possessive
logics, illustrating its connections to settler subjectivity.

Building on Ahmed and Rifkin's account of the orientated nature of
settler standpoints, we have argued that [settlers inhabit an
orientation towards White sovereignty]{.underline}. This orientation is
[produced against the background of race]{.underline}, and is [premised
on the elimination of Indigenous sovereignty]{.underline}. We argue that
[**preconceived theories of race** underlie White settler colonial
studies' description of settler colonialism. White settler colonial
studies' accounts of the settler/Indigenous binary, logic of
elimination, and settler sovereignty **present settlers as
transhistorical subjects** by taking the logics of race and White
possessive sovereignty as given. This]{.underline} analysis [deepens the
critiques of White settler colonial studies as a discipline that
presents settler colonialism as an **impenetrable** and **determined
structure**]{.underline}, not to discard White settler colonial studies'
theoretical contributions, but to theorise the preconceived conditions
of race that limit the discipline.

By [restoring Indigenous sovereignty to the foreground]{.underline},
Cultural Interface theory [reveals possibilities]{.underline} that
emerge from [**beyond the limits of White settler colonial studies**. It
suggests]{.underline} that [we might **direct our attention to the
possibilities for decolonisation that already exist**]{.underline}.
Thinking in terms of orientations opens up multiple possibilities shaped
by the direction one faces, **[without abstracting the subject from the
social and political relations in which they are enmeshed and
implicated. It]{.underline}** theoretically **[restores agency to
settlers and non-Indigenous peoples]{.underline}**; it [allows for them
to make decisions to turn away from White sovereignty and reorient
themselves towards Indigenous sovereignty, **without claiming to be
either Indigenous or innocent**]{.underline}. By virtue of their
position on sovereign Indigenous land, [non-Indigenous peoples
may]{.underline} also [be written into another set of
relations]{.underline}, namely [in relation to Indigenous peoples and
their world views]{.underline}.101 To quote Ahmed,

> Depending on which way one turns, different worlds might even come
> into view. If such turns are repeated over time, then bodies acquire
> the very shape of such direction.102

### Theory Wrong\-\--No Water Ontology\-\--2AC

#### Ontology is [backwards]{.underline}\-\--there's no singular ontological theory of water.

**Boelens et al. 24**, Richard Boelens, Special Professor of the
Political Ecology of Water at the University of Amsterdam Centre for
Latin American Research and Documentation, Personal Professor of Water
Governance and Social Justice at Wageningen University, Ph.D. from
Wageningen University; Lena Hommes, Lecturer and Postdoctoral Researcher
in the Department Geography and the Institute of the Environment at the
University of Girona, Ph.D. from Wageningen University; Jaime
