Hoogesteger, Associate Professor of Water Commons and Collectives at
Wageningen University, Ph.D. in Water Governance from Wageningen
University, "Ontological Politics in River Defence Debates: Unpacking
Fields of Contention in Eco-Centric and Non-Human Turns," Water
Alternatives, Vol. 17, No. 3, 2024, accessed 06-28-2025,
https://www.water-alternatives.org/index.php/alldoc/articles/vol17/v17issue3/762-a17-3-4/file,
DG

[Ontologies are modes of conceptualising and ordering the world,
providing a means by which we can imagine, explain, live with, and act
upon]{.underline} \'[the world we are and live in]{.underline}\'. They
are sets of concepts and categories that help us identify, assemble,
order, and explain particular entities. Thereby, ontologies are linked
to imaginary worlds but also to concrete practices, as well as to forms
of engagement with these socio-ecological entities. They are
simultaneously of an ethical, social, symbolic, and material nature.
[Ontologies allow us to make sense of]{.underline} socio-ecological
[entities' nature and properties]{.underline}, their [interrelated
constituent parts, and the relationships that give them substance and
meaning]{.underline} (Boelens et al., 2023). Therefore, ontological
constitution and practices include the process of material-political
translation from the imaginary to material becoming.

In our subject case, ontological river imaginaries may become
materialised in concrete infrastructure projects or other
territorialisation endeavours (see Hommes et al., 2022; Manosalvas et
al., 2023). Their [materialisation]{.underline}, however, [never
emerges]{.underline} \'[out of the blue]{.underline}\' [but is
historically and socio-materially rooted in cultural-political
contexts]{.underline}. As Hommes et al. (2022: 3-4) point out, it is
also importantly \"(...) contingent upon a group's ability to mobilize
the necessary political, cultural, intellectual, financial, and/or
physical- coercive power\". Thus, from a Foucauldian perspective,
onto-epistemological struggles go further than mere encounters over
patterned cultural signs, words, language, myths, conceptualisations, or
bodies of meaning. [Onto-epistemological struggles]{.underline}
necessarily include sociotechnical practices and modes of socio- natural
configuration. They [connect knowledge, power, and truth in particular
ways]{.underline}, in order [to achieve and legitimise a certain
disposition of actors, material tools, artefacts, ecologies,
and]{.underline} the human and non- human [relations that sustain
them]{.underline} (Foucault, 1980). Accordingly, river ontological
materialisation projects must be understood as struggles over
imaginaries and the associated identities, subjectivities, and meanings
that concern the wished-for hydrosocial territorial order (Boelens et
al., 2016).

Departing from this notion, [we approach river ontologies
as]{.underline} (conscious and unconscious) Foucauldian [intentions
to]{.underline} \"[tame the wild profusion of existing
things]{.underline}\". This implies that [we need to **challenge
place-based truths** as well as universal **representations that presume
to know the nature of reality**]{.underline}. In the water world, these
can express themselves as, for example, \'modernist H 2O\',
functionalist \'river eco-systems\', \'rivers as intrinsic rights
entities\', or \'rivers as sentient beings\' (e.g. Wilson and Inkster,
2018; Bourguignon, 2023; Flaminio and Reynard, 2023; Turska and Ludwig,
2023; Mosquera-Guerrero and Krueger, 2024). Consequently, beyond
interrogations regarding either \'fiction or reality\', we are
interested in how truth regimes work and how ontological understandings
are mobilised, used, fixed, and devised by dominant and/or subaltern
groups.

In his essay El idioma analítico de John Wilkins, Jorge Luis Borges
(1942) playfully analysed the taxonomy of the world fictitiously taken
from an \"unknown Chinese encyclopaedia\" entitled the Celestial
Emporium of Benevolent Knowledge. Borges (1942: 3) describes the Chinese
encyclopaedia:

> In its remote pages it is written that animals are divided into: (a)
> belonging to the Emperor, (b) embalmed, (c) tame, (d) suckling pigs,
> (e) sirens, (f) fabled, (g) stray dogs, (h) included in the present
> classification, (i) frenzied, (j) innumerable, (k) drawn with a very
> fine camelhair brush, (l) et cetera, (m) having just broken the water
> pitcher, (n) that from a long way off look like flies.

Borges takes a pragmatic approach to the arbitrariness of finding
universal frames for analysing and describing reality: \"(...) [it is
clear that there is **no classification of the universe that is not
arbitrary and full of conjectures**. The reason for this is very simple:
**we do not know what the universe is**]{.underline}\".
[This]{.underline} observation [sets the stage for ontological
constructions amidst diversities and insecurities]{.underline}. \"The
impossibility of penetrating the divine scheme of the universe cannot
stop us from planning human schemes, even though we are aware that they
are provisional\" (Borges 1942: 3).

Foucault deploys the Borgesian joke to analyse, first, how the heart of
the matter is not the strangeness of individual categories, but the
unimaginability of this -- and other -- mode(s) of taxonomic ordering.1
Second, he shows how existing epistemes, ontologies, and systems of
rationality determine our mindset, as well as how knowledge and truth
could be ordered quite differently. The [wonderment about other systems
of thought]{.underline}, as imposed by Borges' fables, [challenges the
limitations of our own mode of thinking]{.underline}. As Descola (2014:
271) elaborates, \"(...) it is time that we endeavour to understand how
they \[[other styles of worlding]{.underline}\] [are composed without
automatic recourse to our own mode of composition]{.underline}; it is
time that we set out to recompose them so as to make them more amenable
to a wider variety of inhabitants, human and nonhuman\" (2014: 279).
Foucault (1980, 1994) emphasises that dominant power/knowledge regimes
often prevent or obliterate alternative ways of thinking and acting. We
therefore have to question our own modes of categorising, organising,
and conceptualising, and the powers that support them.

This makes it essential to consider the frames that claim to know the
\'nature of nature\' -- or in our case the \'nature of rivers\' -- as
relational. These [frames are created, sustained, and transformed in
specific]{.underline} social, technological, and natural
[contexts]{.underline}, which are **[always and necessarily politically
constituted]{.underline}**. Consequently, [river ontologies need to be
seen as construed through ordering modes and models that create a very
specific disposition of things]{.underline}
(nature/technology/humans/non-humans) and the relationships that sustain
and give meaning to them (e.g. Hoogesteger et al., 2016; Rogers and
Crow- Miller, 2017; Aubriot et al., 2017; Paerregaard, 2018; Wilson,
2019; Yates, 2022; Macpherson et al., 2024; O'Donnell et al., 2024;
Rickard and Ludwig, 2024).

Thus, [at the heart of struggles for control over rivers is a struggle
to establish or transform \'socio- natural order(s)\' and the modes of
knowing them]{.underline}, which can be accomplished through the
triangulation of knowledge, power, and truth. [Whenever scientific,
policy, vernacular, or indigenous ontologies and models for
explaining]{.underline} river realities and [water worlds are portrayed
as \'real\']{.underline} (rather than entrances to imagining the real),
[the power-laden ordering agreements, choices, interpretations, and
meanings are concealed and naturalised]{.underline}. This, we highlight,
is highly problematic, as it veils the intrinsically political character
of these ontologies.

The important contribution of the more-than-human, ecocentric, and
de-colonising perspectives is that they demonstrate precisely the
political and social character of the water world's material,
ontological, and epistemological constructions. They show how a
distinction between nature and society is a deeply political construct
and how misrecognising this (in mainstream academia, policy formulation,
\'development\' interventions, and public discourse) in and of itself is
a fundamentally political act that suffocates other representations
(e.g. Blaser, 2013; Escobar, 2018; Kothari et al., 2019). Therefore, our
critique is not directed at how [ontological turn theorists]{.underline}
have helped to break open the mono-ontological fixes. Quite the
opposite! Instead, we question how much of their [application in
everyday policy]{.underline}, including activist debates, globalising
policy discourses, and popularised alternative approaches [may in fact
attempt to **re-fix ontologies, re-naturalise the society/nature
distinction, and de-politicise nature framings**]{.underline} (see also
Bessire and Bond, 2014; Rawson and Mansfield, 2018). We elaborate this
below through the identification of five distinct fields of contention
in new ontological turns, as expressed in river debates.

### Theory Wrong\-\--Posthumanism\-\--2AC

#### Posthumanist critiques of humanism aren't mutually exclusive with contingent embrace of its benefits. 

Guillermo Díaz de **Liaño &** Manuel **Fernández-Götz 21**, Field
Archaeologist at the AOC Archaeology Group, Ph.D. in Archaeology from
the University of Edinburgh; Professor of Later European Prehistory at
St. Cross College, University of Oxford, Ph.D. from Christian-Albrecht
University Kiel, "Posthumanism, New Humanism and Beyond," Cambridge
Archaeological Journal, Vol. 31, No. 3, August 2021, accessed
07-05-2025, https://doi.org/10.1017/S095977432100024X, DG

We believe that a [complete rejection of Humanism is **unproductive**
and potentially **dangerous**]{.underline}. To start with,
[critics]{.underline} of Humanism [should acknowledge that this term
**does not designate a homogeneous concept**]{.underline}. Fassin
(2019), for example, distinguishes three major lineages in its
genealogy, which he designates as Humanism I, II and III. [As
Posthumanism is an umbrella term that encompasses multiple approaches,
**so too is Humanism**: neither]{.underline} of them [should be
oversimplified or disregarded]{.underline} in their entirety. Although
the [complete rejection of]{.underline} core elements of [the Western
world]{.underline} (e.g. Humanism, Modernity) [might sound appealing in
an academic world that]{.underline} often **[fetishizes theoretical
'newness']{.underline}** (Ribeiro 2016b), [novel reflexive critiques
could]{.underline} often [benefit from integrating elements of
Modernity's legacy of knowledge and experience]{.underline}
(Criado-Boado 2016, 157; Preucel, this volume).

It is worth keeping in mind that posthumanist perspectives are
reflecting wider trends in society that are inextricably linked to the
rapid growth of artificial intelligence (AI) and biotechnology, which
are in the course of diluting the boundaries between humans and
non-humans in ways that we can still not fully comprehend (Barrat 2013;
Lanier 2014; Wolff 2017). Moreover, [the rise of Posthumanism is taking
place at the same time that the Humanities are increasingly under
attack]{.underline} on a global scale, with dramatic cuts in funding and
reduced social appreciation, which poses a direct threat to critical
thinking and, ultimately, democracy (Nussbaum 2010; Trepanier 2018).
While heading into an unknown future, perhaps [we should keep in mind
some of the core values of Humanism in a **philosophical** and **ethical
sense**]{.underline} (e.g. Wolff 2010; 2017; Zuboff 2019).

Thus, [instead of rejecting Humanism]{.underline} altogether [and
**throwing the baby out with the bathwater**, it is worth reflecting on
the call for a New Humanism]{.underline} that has recently been proposed
in philosophical anthropology by authors such as Wentzer and Mattingly
(2018) and Simonsen (2012). According to their perspective, this would
imply an approach that

> is [not committed to religious or metaphysical claims concerning human
> essence or human superiority]{.underline}. It does not appeal to a
> secular antireligious cultural movement, nor to a developmental stage
> in (Western-dominated) human civilization. Rather, our proposal marks
> a commitment to deal with 'the human' as a common and indispensable
> denominator for the ontological and ethical domains of anthropology
> and adjacent disciplines. (Wentzer & Mattingly 2018, 146)

Viewed from this angle, [New Humanism is both a critique of some of the
dangers of traditional Humanism, and also a call for caring about humans
in a world increasingly facing dehumanization]{.underline} (Porpora
2017), [while]{.underline} at the same time [committing to global
sustainability]{.underline} (Bokova 2010; D'Orville 2015).

In what concerns archaeology, [we argue for an approach]{.underline}
that is [focused on the study of the human past, not]{.underline} in
order [to reinforce obsolete notions of]{.underline}
'[superiority]{.underline}' [or]{.underline} '[progress]{.underline}',
[but to understand the **non-linear, multivocal and multifaceted
diversity of human experiences**]{.underline} in time and space **[and
its interrelatedness with non-human entities]{.underline}**. We consider
that [the presence of the human is an **imperative**]{.underline} if we
are doing archaeology, [but we understand that humanness is constituted
and performed differently]{.underline} through time and space, thus
[including a diverse and changing array of non-human entities and
relationships]{.underline}. Archaeology, from this perspective, is not
the 'discipline of things', but rather the study of humans through
things and in relation to things.

Grasping the complexity and diversity of the past requires a variety of
theoretical approaches and viewpoints, and most archaeologists already
employ a range of perspectives to achieve this. [**Fluid approaches**
that allow for different theoretical elements to be assembled seem
**more inclusive** and **fruitful than** establishing **rigid divides
between pro- and anti-humanist approaches**]{.underline}. Thus, [some
**posthumanist perspectives can be incorporated by authors
who**]{.underline}, as we do, **[see value in maintaining the centrality
of humans]{.underline}** within archaeological studies. Perhaps, as
Fassin (2019, 37) suggests, [a post-post-humanism]{.underline} will
develop in the near future, which

> [would not be a]{.underline} mere [return to the varieties of humanism
> that we have known, with their historical flaws and ethical
> ambiguities, but would **affirm the categorical imperative of a
> critical approach to human worlds**]{.underline} in a time
> **[when]{.underline}** they are **[faced with multiple menaces that
> affect both humans and nonhumans]{.underline}** \[. . .\].
> [This]{.underline} post-post-humanism [would remind us that much of
> what happens to human beings and to the world]{.underline} that they
> inhabit is the result of human actions and therefore **[involves human
> responsibility]{.underline}**---[notwithstanding the ambiguity of the
> word human]{.underline}.

## Impact

### No Root Cause\-\--Imperialism\-\--2AC

#### Saying Western imperialism is the root of all evil sanctions violence by both Western and non-Western actors.

Dorothy **Figueria 8**, Professor of Comparative Literature at the
University of Georgia, Ph.D. in Comparative Studies in Literature from
the University of Chicago, "Occidentalism," in *Otherwise Occupied:
Pedagogies of Alterity and the Brahminization of Theory*, pp. 91-106,
State University of New York Press, 2008, accessed 06-27-2025, ProQuest,
DG

Postcolonialism added to this critique its own universal based on
Orientalist criticism: **[Western imperialism is always to
blame]{.underline}**. As Edward Rothstein opined in the New York Times,
[any act against the West by a postcolonial power cannot be viewed as
anything but a **reaction** to a previous imperial act by the West. We
cannot]{.underline} then [condemn the World Trade Center attack, since
Western hegemonic behavior is the]{.underline} fundamental [cause of
terrorism, and the **U**nited **S**tates]{.underline}, against which
this act was directed, [is the most powerful Western hegemonic
power]{.underline}. The rhetoric of postcolonialism and its call for
[the rule of relativism enabled us to **blame the victim** and **justify
terror**]{.underline}. Rothstein, a mere journalist, questioned whether
such relativism should play any role in the face of destruction that
called for a \"transcendent ethical perspective\" (Rothstein 2001). We,
as readers of recent literary criticism, are usually far removed from
such moral concerns. In fact, we might even find the logic in play here
commonplace and conventional. What could be a more disturbing indictment
of the state of theory today?

The [Occidentalist rhetoric]{.underline} exhibited in these responses
reflects the school of thought that [views the **U**nited **S**tates as
the **source of all evils**]{.underline} ([from the price of beef in
France to AIDS in Africa]{.underline}). This world vision offers certain
advantages. For one, [it allows some countries **to avoid accepting
responsibility for their actions**]{.underline} (Revel 2003:61). [By
attributing **all moral failings** and **grotesque intellectual errors**
to the **U**nited **S**tates as the **monster scapegoat**]{.underline},
certain [nations can **absolve themselves** of their own moral errors
and mistakes. If America becomes **the Great Satan, all the little
Satans pale in comparison**. America can]{.underline} thus exist to
[console]{.underline} Europe and [the world for its own
failures]{.underline} (Revel 2003: 158---59) [among which we can count
its **complacency and**]{.underline} indulgent [**complicity
in**]{.underline} Communist and post-Communist
**[genocides]{.underline}**.

### No Impact\-\--Materiality Outweighs\-\--2AC

#### Vote aff [even if]{.underline} they win their theory\-\--extinction is categorically distinct from any ontology argument.

Jonathan **Howard 20**, Assistant Professor of English and African
American Studies at Yale University, Ph.D. in English from Duke
University, "To See the Earth before the End of the Antiblack World,"
Souls, Vol. 22, No. 2-4, 2020, accessed 06-28-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1080/10999949.2021.2003622, DG

I've said I want to speak a word of black life; and no one has cleared
more theoretical ground for such a word, and so, for the skewed study of
black life as a genuine largeness, than Fred Moten. While certainly no
stranger to black death or antiblackness, Moten's black study is a party
more often than it isn't. He makes the bias of his black study plain
when he outlines his desire in Black and Blur to "subordinate by a
measure so small that it constitutes measure's eclipse, the critical
analysis of anti-blackness to the celebratory analysis of
blackness."Footnote49 Jared Sexton once heard Moten state "measure's
eclipse" numerically, detailing in "The Social Life of Social Death" how
[Moten]{.underline} once "[remarked to the effect]{.underline} that [he
always wants his writing to be comprised of 49.99% critique and 50.01%
celebration]{.underline}."Footnote50 [This delineation of black study
into the "celebration of blackness" and the "critique of anti-blackness"
yields another compelling example of black ambivalence]{.underline}. And
just as we observed of the cooperation between the testaments of black
life and death in Sharpe's black study[, the "critique of
anti-blackness" and "celebration of blackness" are]{.underline}, for
Moten, [not oppositional, but complementary operations that combine to
**produce black study**]{.underline}. Thus, he explains that the "rich,
rigorous, powerful, and utterly necessary analytic of anti-blackness"
actually enables the celebration of blackness; and that this
celebration, in turn, is "done not to avoid or ameliorate the hard
truths of anti-blackness but in the service of its violent
eradication."Footnote51

This delineation of black study offers a framework that bonds the
witness of our forked-tongued tradition together. But beyond serving as
just another example of black ambivalence, the subordination of critique
to celebration in Moten's black study also raises important questions
about the nature of the relationship between blackness and
anti-blackness. That there could be even marginally more blackness to
celebrate than antiblackness to critique pulls blackness out from under
anti-'s totalizing shadow. It leads us to question whether
"anti-blackness" is the first, only, and exhaustive word of black study,
whether the only blackness to speak of is that which gathers under
anti-'s orthographic shadow. When we take part in "the devotional
practice that is given in recitation of the sentence 'blackness is x,'"
have we been misspelling blackness?Footnote52 Leaving out a silent
"anti-"? Is there any word we can speak for blackness that does not
always already presume this prefix, which is nothing but the grammatical
sign of blackness' existence within what Frank Wilderson has theorized
as a broader "grammar of suffering"?Footnote53 [Does blackness have any
life outside of the anti-black "world]{.underline} that was going to
kill" Equiano?

Of course, in .02%, we are talking about a black lining thinner than
silver's. But razor thin as it is, its sheer excess is enough to put
significant pressure on the understanding of antiblackness as that which
"precedes and exceeds" blackness. It introduces the possibility that
this apparent relation can, by another frame, appear inverted, such that
[blackness rather "**precedes and exceeds**" anti-blackness, and is
**not diminished** by anti-blackness' capacity to occlude. By
establishing blackness]{.underline} (and by extension black life) [as
excessive, the .02% margin by which Moten subordinates the "critique of
anti-blackness" to the "celebration of blackness" models and
invites]{.underline} such [ambivalence as is **ultimately skewed** in
the direction of black life]{.underline}.Footnote54 But what grounds are
there to justify this sense of "the largeness that is black life?" And
are they quite so substantial as that which the antiblack world
furnishes to the thought of black death?

Perhaps [we can pursue the grounds of .02%]{.underline} in the nearly
nothing interval of "soon" that elapses between Equiano's initial
"astonishment" at the sight of an unknown ocean and his "terror" upon
boarding the slave ship.

> The first object which saluted my eyes when I arrived on the coast was
> the sea, and a slave ship, which was then riding at anchor, and
> waiting for its cargo. These filled me with astonishment, which was
> soon converted into terror when I was carried on board. I was
> immediately handled and tossed up to see if I were sound by some of
> the crew; and I was now persuaded that I had gotten into a world of
> bad spirits, and that they were going to kill me.Footnote55

Notwithstanding the terror of the antiblack world that was going to kill
him, I am struck that the very "first thing that saluted" Equiano, even
prior to the slave ship, was the sea. And that when he saw it, he was
astonished. That this astonishment was "soon converted to terror," in a
conversion which mirrors Equiano's own conversion into cargo---and thus,
the Black subject of the nonhuman hold---suggests an interval so short
as to be almost negligible. Especially when considered beside the sheer
scale of the antiblack world, and all there is proportionally to say
about something on the scale of a world when held up against a moment,
here and already gone. The agenda for black study that Equiano's record
of the beginning would seem to set is the study, critique, and
overturning of this world. And I am certainly about that life. But here
is where I stumble, felled perhaps too long in the passing interval of
"soon." It's Equiano's "astonishment" that gets me, again and again and
always. That there was, that there could be, astonishment. Then, in the
time of astonishment, for a moment, but only for a moment, the Earth
gathers, dancing.

Equiano did not jump to see the Earth before the end of his world and
the beginning of ours. But he did see it. And he was astonished. Just
prior to being "saluted," or hailed in the Althusserian sense, by the
slave ship---"Hey you there! Hey, cargo!"---to discovering that he
really was the cargo for which the ship was waiting, and in this way
being interpellated as black, Equiano was greeted by the blue face of an
overwhelmingly blue planet. What if, compelled by such astonishment, we
fall deep into the fleeting interval of soon? What would it mean to
think the dawning of blackness in Middle Passage as not reducible to the
"social death" hailed in the salute of the slave ship? What if, instead,
we think blackness as not just responding also, but first, to the
hailing waves of the astonishing sea? "Hey you there! Hey, earthling."
What if, as our original antiphony, blackness dawns in the ongoing
response of black ecological life to the hail of a blue planet?

[The study of black life as a genuine largeness begins when
we]{.underline}, with Equiano and an innumerable cloud of witnesses,
[stop to **behold the Earth before the end of the antiblack
world**]{.underline}. [This Earth still hails and is hardly as much
world as it appears on its surface]{.underline}, which is so much more
ocean than not. Or else do we suppose that whiteness has managed to
straightjacket the planet so utterly that its definition is no longer
possibility, as the narrator of Invisible Man once averred?Footnote56

If, as Ed Roberson explains, the "nature poem occurs when an
individual's sense of the larger Earth enters into the world of human
knowledge," and if the "main understanding that results from this
encounter is...that the world's desires do not run the Earth, but the
Earth does run the world,"Footnote57 then what would it mean for a
larger sense of the Earth to enter black studies? Or, to invoke a
beautiful line of Roberson's poetry that I can't stop thinking about,
how might we endeavor, "to see the Earth before the end of the
\[antiblack\] world?"Footnote58 And what would happen to our
understandings of black life as that which is insisted from the
totalizing grounds of black death, if we shift our critical frame from
the world to the Earth, as our era of climate crisis seems to demand?
Indeed, [if by "social death," black studies]{.underline} has [arrived
at its most complete understanding of what it means to be black in and
relative to the world, it is yet left to the field to attend ever more
robustly to what it means to be **black on and in relationship to the
Earth, that exceeds the world**. The necessity of this vector of black
study is all the more apparent in the light of]{.underline} a [**global
ecological crisis** that has illuminated the]{.underline} (Human)
[social **life of the world as a still deeper death**]{.underline}. So,
[if what we want is not only apocalypse, but an apocalypse we might live
in, then **even more than social death, we must learn to study and
celebrate what**]{.underline}, by another frame, **[can be perceived as
the largeness that is black ecological life. Because after the end of
the world, there will be black people on Earth, or none at
all]{.underline}**.

### No Impact\-\--Humanism\-\--2AC

#### The 'human' is not collapsing, and posthumanism is conceptually useless.

Simon **Susen 22**, Professor of Sociology at City St. George's,
University of London, Ph.D. from the University of Cambridge,
"Reflections on the (Post-)Human Condition: Towards New Forms of
Engagement with the World?", Social Epistemology, Vol. 36, No. 1, 2022,
accessed 07-05-2025, https://doi.org/10.1080/02691728.2021.1893859, DG

Given that Braidotti remains firmly embedded in a poststructuralist
framework, it is far from clear why she wishes to hold on to the concept
of 'the subject'. Granted, owing to the Foucauldian presuppositions
underpinning her approach, [it makes sense]{.underline} for her [to
advocate a posthumanist]{.underline} and post-anthropocentric
[conception of]{.underline} '[subjectivity]{.underline}'. Indeed, as she
affirms, '\[b\]y "posthumanizing" subjectivity, it can be re-positioned
as a dynamic convergence phenomenon across the contradictions of
posthumanism and post-anthropocentrism'.240 Thus, subjectivity exists
within a spatiotemporally contingent field of tensions. More
specifically, '[posthuman subjectivity]{.underline}'241 can be conceived
of as 'a process of becoming in its own immanence and not in binary
oppositional terms'.242 It [is in a **permanent state of
flux**]{.underline}, and [its multilayered constitution does not follow
a binary, let alone monolithic, logic of functioning]{.underline}. At
the same time, it is irreducible to the 'Homo Universalis of
Humanism'243 and the 'Anthropos of anthropocentrism',244 since it is 'a
structural relational capacity'245 that defies the rigid logic of a
fixed sense of 'human nature' or 'human essence'. For Braidotti,
subjectivity is 'both post-personal and pre-individual',246 in that it
finds itself situated in a dynamic, and largely unpredictable, process
of 'constant negotiation with multiple others and immersed in the
conditions that it is trying to understand and modify, if not
overturn'.247 On this view, amo[ng the key features of human
subjectivity are malleability, negotiability, unpredictability,
multiplicity, contingency, and]{.underline} -- above all --
[relationality]{.underline}.

A noteworthy problem with this account of subjectivity, however, is
that, [despite its advocate's **nominally anti-essentialist stance**, it
**relies on effectively essentialist**]{.underline} -- or at least
quasi-essentialist -- **[assumptions about]{.underline}** the very
nature of **[what it means to be]{.underline}**
'**[human]{.underline}**'. For the aforementioned charac[teristics are
**constitutive elements of any human creature**]{.underline} -- that is,
they are traits that are inherent in our species-specific condition.

[This reflection takes us to]{.underline} Braidotti's conception of
'[the subject]{.underline}' [in general]{.underline} and her conception
of 'the posthuman subject' in particular. She maintains that
'\[p\]osthuman subjects are a work-in- progress'248 and that,
furthermore, 'they emerge as both a critical and a creative project
[within the posthuman convergence]{.underline} along posthumanist and
post-anthropocentric axes of interrogation'.249 One may legitimately
object, however, that at least the first part of this statement applies
not only to (post-)human but also to non-human subjects. [By definition,
all living beings are]{.underline} '[a work-in-progress]{.underline}',
[in]{.underline} the sense [that they are **constantly developing
and**]{.underline}, as spatiotemporally situated beings, [responding to
different, and incessantly evolving, environments and historical
circumstances]{.underline}.

This leads us to another issue, namely the question of 'we'. Who are
'we'?250 Braidotti claims that posthuman subjects 'explore the
multifaceted and differential nature of the collective "we"'.251 In
other words, the 'we' to which she is referring cannot be reduced to a
monolithic, let alone transcendental, entity; rather, given its
relational constitution, we are dealing with a multilayered and highly
differentiated form of 'we', whose posthuman constitution is indicative
of the posthuman era. According to Braidotti, '"\[w\]e" are in the
process of becoming posthumanist and post- anthropocentric'.252 We do so
as embodied and embedded selves, since 'we are deeply steeped in the
material world'253; and we do so as transversal selves, since 'we
connect \[with\] but also differ from each other'.254 Bonded by our
'ontological relationality',255 we are -- in vitalist terms --
'variations on a common matter'.256 Yet, paradoxically, 'we differ from
each other all the more as we co-define ourselves within the same living
matter -- environmentally, socially, and relationally'.257 [Just as we
share our ontological relationality with all other living beings, we
differ from each other due to our context-dependent
specificities]{.underline}.

One of the main problems with this approach, however, is that [the
notion of]{.underline} '[we]{.underline}' that lies at its core [is both
**vague** and **presumptuous**]{.underline}: it is vague because [it is
not clear whether it comprises all or only some living
beings]{.underline}; at the same time, [it is presumptuous because the
contention that we are all in the process of becoming
posthumanist]{.underline} and post-anthropocentric [is **far-fetched**.
If we include non-human agents]{.underline} in our definition of 'the
subject', then [the question arises whether all]{.underline} zoe-based
[beings]{.underline} (including animals, seeds, cells, and plants) --
and, indeed, all geo- and techno-based forms of existence -- [can be
included]{.underline} in this category. If the answer is 'yes', then we
are faced with an inflationary conception of 'the subject', which, owing
to its denotative elasticity, fails to do justice to the
species-constitutive distinctiveness of human life.

Of course, one may come to Braidotti's defence by insisting that one of
the principal aims of her project is to undo the boundaries between
different kinds of 'subject' and 'subjectivity' (notably 'human',
'inhuman', 'posthuman', and 'non-human') and that, in some instances,
the 'we' she envisages is more or less clearly defined.258 After all,
she urges us to 'think of ourselves as planetary subjects, rather than
as global agents'.259 Crucially, the types of subject she has in mind
include both 'the human and \[the\] inhuman inhabitants of this
planet'.260 [If]{.underline}, however, [we]{.underline} -- effectively
-- [include **every form of existence**]{.underline} (that is,
'[human']{.underline}, '[inhuman']{.underline},
'[posthuman']{.underline}, [and 'non-human']{.underline}) in our
definition of 'subject' and 'subjectivity', then [these terms **lose
conceptual force**, since, in an omni-onto-epistemological fashion,
**they refer to everything and nothing**]{.underline}.

[Even if]{.underline}, in a narrow sense, [we reserve the]{.underline}
concept of 'the [subject]{.underline}' [for members of our species, it
seems erroneous to assume that all human beings are]{.underline} going
through the process of [being converted into posthuman(ist)]{.underline}
and/or post-anthropocentric [actors]{.underline}. Ironically, the
intersectional divisions to which Braidotti rightly draws her readers'
attention are the very reason that -- to use a modified version of an
Orwellian aphorism -- some human actors are more (or less)
posthuman(ist) and/or post-anthropocentric than others. [Human actors
are **divided by key sociological variables**]{.underline} ([such as
class, ethnicity, gender, age, and 'ability']{.underline}). [Our
position in the world, including our access to socially relevant
resources, is **shaped by these variables**]{.underline}. Braidotti's
[critical theory]{.underline} of society [needs to emphasize the fact
that the degree to which actors have]{.underline} ([or have
not]{.underline}) [become posthuman(ist)]{.underline} and/or
post-anthropocentric [**depends on** the **social positions** they
occupy and the social dispositions they acquire within the social
universe. Otherwise, the]{.underline} bold [proposition that we have
been witnessing the rise of 'a new collective subject]{.underline}, a
"we-are-(all)-in-this-together-but-we-are-not-one-and-the- same" kind of
subject',261 [is **of merely rhetorical, rather than substantive,
relevance**]{.underline}.

## Alternative

### Alt Fails\-\--Decoloniality\-\--2AC

#### The alternative's epistemic focus fails\-\--it uncritically embraces non-Western ideas and re-orients histories around overly romanticized notions of colonial struggle at the expense of meaningful emancipation. 

Ilan **Kapoor 24**, Professor of Critical Development Studies at the
York University Faculty of Environmental and Urban Change, Ph.D. in
Political Science from the University of Toronto, "Intersectionality,
Decoloniality, Indigenous Localism: A Critique," Theory, Culture &
Society, OnlineFirst, 12-24-2024, accessed 06-27-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1177/02632764241303689, DG

[Decoloniality tends to approach contemporary politics in **stark,
black-and-white terms**]{.underline}, broadly [rejecting Western
modernity, while **valorizing decolonial**]{.underline} (and especially
Global South and subaltern) **[ways of knowing and
living]{.underline}**. For Mignolo (2007), on whose work I focus here,
the geo-political location of knowledge and identity matters (p. 469).
This is because, for him, European modernity has been coercively imposed
on the rest of the world as universal panacea (Mignolo, 2018: xii). His
goal, instead, is to retrieve a 'pluriverse' of decolonized subaltern
epistemologies.

Central to Mignolo's viewpoint is Quijano's (2000, 2007) notion of
'coloniality of power' -- the systematic application of material,
epistemic, and aesthetic resources needed to ensure empire. Here
[coloniality is seen as continuing]{.underline} in the former colonies
[even after]{.underline} their [political independence, manifesting as
the pervasiveness of Eurocentric knowledge systems and]{.underline}
continuing [patterns of racial oppression and socioeconomic
exploitation]{.underline}. For Mignolo, coloniality cannot be separated
from Western modernity; both are co-constitutive: '"modernity" is a
complex narrative . . . that builds Western civilization by celebrating
its achievements while hiding at the same time its darker side,
"coloniality". Coloniality . . . is constitutive of modernity -- there
is no modernity without coloniality' (Mignolo, 2011: 2--3). The
(post)colonial subject is accordingly only peripherally incorporated
into the Euro-North American metropole, with subaltern epistemologies
either wiped out or classified as 'underdeveloped' in relation to
European norms (Mignolo, 2012: xxv).

In response, Mignolo (2011) advocates for 'epistemic disobedience and
delinking from the colonial matrix in order to open up decolonial
options' (pp. 9, 54). His aim is decolonizing the colonial subject's
imaginary by fracturing 'the hegemony of knowledge and understanding
that have been ruled, since the fifteenth century . . . \[by\] the
theological and ego-logical politics of knowledge and understanding'
(Mignolo, 2010: 313). For him, this epistemological shift will help
transform both the terms and the content of the coloniality/modernity
matrix. Mignolo draws on Anzaldúa (1987) to argue for 'border thinking',
through which suppressed subaltern cosmologies are transformed into
resources for constructing 'alternatives to modernity' (Mignolo, 2010:
320, 2011: xxviii, 2012: 12, 157).

It is such epistemological delinking that, according to Mignolo, will
help produce decolonial 'pluriversality'. Here he borrows the well-known
Zapatista motto of creating '"another world" in which many worlds will
co-exist' to make the case for a planet of multiple co-existing
ontologies and temporalities. The idea is to enable, not a single global
future dictated by the West, but plural ones in which decoloniality
would be one option among others, including what he names as
'Rewesternization' (through which Western powers attempt to maintain
their declining global political-economic power) and 'Dewesternization'
(through which 'emerging powers' challenge Western hegemony) (Mignolo,
2011: 27--35).

A Politics of Authenticity and Disavowal

There is much to learn from decoloniality -- from its multi-dimensional
analysis of coloniality, to its critique of Western modernity and its
prescriptions for decolonizing the colonial matrix. But Mignolo's
[analysis inclines toward **romanticizing decolonial
spaces**]{.underline}. He [has]{.underline} almost [**nothing critical
to say** about the subaltern]{.underline}/Global South; [it is as though
they are somehow **immune to contradiction, failure, or
disagreement**]{.underline} (Kapoor and Zalloua, 2022: 96--9).

[This is evident in]{.underline} Mignolo's [reliance on **simplified**
and **intransigent binaries**]{.underline} such as when he pits
[Western]{.underline} '[imperial]{.underline}' [universalism against
decolonial pluriversality]{.underline}, which for him is invariably
'convivial, dialogical, or plurilogical . . . decolonial and communal
personalities are driven by the search for love, conviviality, and
harmony' (Mignolo, 2018: xii, xiv; see also Mignolo, 2011: 313). It is
also evident in the work of the Modernity/Coloniality/Decoloniality
research programme, a collective Latin America- focused research project
of which both Mignolo and Arturo Escobar are part. The project
prioritizes 'non-Eurocentric' forms of living/knowledge rooted in such
communities as the Zapatistas in Mexico, Indigenous groups in Ecuador
and Bolivia, and Afro-Latinx movements in Colombia (Asher, 2013: 838;
see also Escobar, 2020: 84--96, 120--35; Mignolo, 2011: 11, 213--51).
Yet when Mignolo applauds, for example, the Zapatistas for their
participatory politics and collective consensus-making, he appears to
ignore that [consensus-based politics can coerce single decisions on
communities]{.underline}, often [suppressing subaltern
voices]{.underline} (Kapoor, 2008: 107--8, 2020: 147--69; see Rancière,
1999). [When Mignolo praises Indigenous communities, he
ignores]{.underline}, for instance, [the **cooptation of Indigenous
leaders** by the Ecuadorean state]{.underline}, the Morales regime's
backtracking on commitments to Indigenous self-determination to appease
capitalist extraction, [**ideological divisions** between and among
Indigenous groups]{.underline} across North, South, and Central America,
or the anti-Muslim politics of sections of adivasi (Aboriginal)
communities in India in collaboration with right-wing Hindu
supremacists: '[What is absent]{.underline} in Mignolo's representations
of the subaltern [are the warnings by]{.underline} the likes of [Freire
and Fanon about the possibility of **internalized subaltern oppression,
racism, and co-optation**]{.underline} (which is ironic, given that
Fanon is one of Mignolo's key decolonial exemplars)' (Kapoor and
Zalloua, 2022: 96; see also Baviskar, 2007; Merino, 2016).

The extent of such an indiscriminate politics of
authenticity/romanticization is to be gleaned in Mignolo's recent
endorsement of a book from a right-wing Hindu author (Deepak, 2021) who
deploys decolonial arguments to make the case for a return to a Hindu
(Brahmanical) epistemology. After a public outcry, Mignolo eventually
withdrew his endorsement, 8 but his willingness to uncritically embrace
a Hindu nationalist politics just because it is disparaging of Western
epistemology and aims at retrieving an 'Indigenous' Hindu cosmology is
revealing of the decolonial tendency to embrace putatively 'authentic'
Global South spaces even if they are elitist or oppressive.

What is conspicuously missing in Mignolo's decolonial position is the
dimension of the Real, the imminent blockage that prevents any
discursive formation -- whether capitalist, post-capitalist, or
pluriversal -- from actualizing its identity. As Crenshaw does with
intersectional identity, Mignolo appears to so revel in the jouissance
of the subaltern/Global South space that he is blind to -- disavows --
social contradictions. Hence his [resort to an authentic pluriversality
devoid of imperfections]{.underline}: a space [where]{.underline}, as
Michaelsen and Shershow (2007) underline in their analysis of his work
(p. 53), [there is de facto consensus and agreement]{.underline}, indeed
[not even the need to criticize, debate, or deliberate]{.underline}.
This [appears as the **textbook definition of ideology**]{.underline} --
**[the erasure of the political, the covering over of social
antagonism]{.underline}** (Žižek, 1989: 25).

The accompanying proclivity is that [the production of an authentic
Global South subaltern space ends up militating against the putative
political subject of decoloniality]{.underline} -- [the
subaltern]{.underline}. In this regard, echoing Spivak (1988), Asher
(2013: 838--9) suggests that [Mignolo and his decoloniality peers
are]{.underline} '[turning to subaltern knowledge to
fulfill]{.underline} \[[their]{.underline}\] [desire to create a just
world]{.underline} for humans and non-humans'. [Decolonial jouissance of
the pluriverse induces the **absence of critical self-reflexivity** on
the part of the decolonial analyst]{.underline}, with the latter
portraying himself as transparent relay able to accurately capture the
subaltern voice. [The search for a subaltern pluriverse]{.underline}
thus [becomes a **self-fulfilling prophecy**: you project a pluriversal
platform on the subaltern and]{.underline} then [rejoice in miraculously
finding it! But in this mêlée, **the subaltern is not
heard**]{.underline} (which is why my above critique of Zapatista
participatory politics says less about the Zapatistas than Mignolo's
construction of them).

Mignolo's Manichaean [argumentation]{.underline} also [tends to be
**broadly dismissive of Western thought**, accusing it of complicity in
the colonial matrix of power]{.underline}, thus appealing for it to be
superseded (Mignolo, 2002: 80, 2010: 346). [He seems to **conflate
hegemonic European**]{.underline} (colonial) **[thought with European
thought writ large]{.underline}**. In so doing, he [fails to
acknowledge]{.underline} the many [European intellectual
counter-discourses to colonial rationality or binary
thinking]{.underline} (anti-imperialism, non-dualism, communitarianism,
etc.) [with which]{.underline}, for all intents and purposes, [much of
decolonial thought is allied]{.underline}. He fails as well to account
for his own debt to so-called Eurocentrism, disavowing that his
intellectual background, as well as the very language (English) and
critical tools he deploys (e.g. geopolitics, difference, epistemology,
gnoseology, aesthesis), are unavoidably Eurocentric (Kapoor and Zalloua,
2022: 99--100). Worse still, his [policing of the boundaries of what
is]{.underline} '[Western'/'non- Western]{.underline}' [ends up eliding
the centuries-old cultural and economic exchange between Europe and its
putative others]{.underline} (Africa, the Arab world, Indigenous
America, the Indian subcontinent, etc.); [or]{.underline} indeed
[the]{.underline} substantial [history of subaltern
resistance]{.underline} -- [in Europe's colonies, and in Europe
itself]{.underline} -- that is integral to the emergence of such notions
as 'equality', 'freedom', and 'social justice' (see, for example,
Anghie, 2005; Buck-Morss, 2009; Cusicanqui, 2012).

Thus, Mignolo's decolonial disavowal of the unconscious/Real is not only
deeply ideological for romanticizing subaltern pluriversality, but also
deeply depoliticizing. Rather than seeing internal (community or social
movement) differences and contradictions as weaknesses/traumas to be
suppressed or avoided in order to produce a perfect subaltern 'hero',
why not try to face them head-on to invent new and creative (although
never harmonious) subaltern political spaces? [Rather than understanding
colonial violence and dispossession as a reason to nostalgically
manufacture past or present glory or **wallow in victimhood and
resentment**]{.underline} (against European thinkers), **[why not
struggle to become proficient with, and use, European discourses of
emancipation and freedom to fight for]{.underline}** subaltern
**[equality and social justice everywhere]{.underline}**? [The Western
colonial legacy is indeed imperialist domination and plunder, but it is
also]{.underline}, as Žižek (2009) underlines, '[that of the
self-critical examination of the violence and exploitation the West
itself brought]{.underline} to the Third World . . . [the West supplied
the very standards by which it]{.underline} ([and its
critics]{.underline}) [measures its own criminal past]{.underline}' (p.
115). **[The antagonisms of European hegemony]{.underline}**, in this
sense, **[can be viewed as powerful subaltern political
resources]{.underline}**. And such is the case not only for the
subaltern from the Global South, but also for the subaltern from the
Global North: their common predicaments could be the occasion to find
cause together in shared trauma (Kapoor, 2020: xv, 66; Kapoor and
Zalloua, 2022: 20). This is something that Mignolo's demonization of the
West precludes, failing as it does to distinguish hegemonic from
subaltern (counter-) discourses, whether in the West or otherwise,
thereby further depoliticizing the subaltern.

Finally, there is the issue of the exclusion of the state and political
economy in Mignolo's gloss. [In regard to the state]{.underline},
Mignolo stands firmly against it, predictably [seeing it as the
political expression of]{.underline} ([Western]{.underline})
[coloniality]{.underline}. For him, pluriversal spaces would 'exist
independently of the state and corporations' by delinking 'from state
forms of governance, from the economy of accumulation' (Mignolo, 2018:
xii, xiv). Thus, 'the future would be composed of "communal nodes"
around the planet cooperating rather than competing with each other . .
. \[through\] the entanglement of several cosmologies connected today in
power differential' (Mignolo, 2011: 283, 2018: x).

But Mignolo's position [appears **politically naive**. Without
some]{.underline} form of [coordinating authority like a state, it
remains unclear how]{.underline} the [security and
well-being]{.underline} of 'communal nodes' [would be realized; what
would ensure **coop**eration, rather than conflict]{.underline}, among
and between nodes, especially [given]{.underline} '[power
differential]{.underline}'; and how these nodes would be able to thwart
the continuing incursion of global capital, or threats from both the
Western and 'de-Westernized' powers envisaged by Mignolo. It seems, in
fact, that his [pluriversal politics **unconsciously requires
a**]{.underline} national, regional, and/or transnational **[state to
ensure the coexistence of]{.underline}** '**[many
worlds']{.underline}**. He appears to count on such things as peace
enforcement, cooperation, provision of universal education and health
care, regulation of multi- nationals, campaigns against global viral
infections, and so forth, for his communal nodes to thrive.

As to political economy, it is all but absent in Mignolo's political
viewpoint, given his pluriversal privileging of epistemological, at the
expense of material, decolonization. He does sketchily mention imperial
resource extraction in his critique of coloniality, but says almost
nothing about the material dimensions of pluriversality. This is because
(expectedly) he assesses Marxist political economy as integral to a
'Eurocentered critique' (Mignolo, 2011: xi, xviii), glossing over the
many variants of Third World Marxisms (whether Nepalese, Cuban, Chinese,
South African, Peruvian, Indian, etc., many of which have been
actualized as political projects with varying degrees of
success/failure, including democratic communism in Kerala and Bengal,
for example). Mignolo (2010) looks instead to 'delinking from
capitalism' epistemologically (p. 339), as though decolonized knowledge
production will undo not just neoliberal ideology but the structures of
global capitalism. The overwhelming evidence to date points to just the
opposite: as mentioned earlier, the [success of corporate capitalism
lies]{.underline} in part [in its cooptation of the
epistemological-cultural claims of social movements]{.underline} --
[because not only do they not pose a threat to accumulation, but they
can]{.underline} also [be commodified to advance
accumulation]{.underline}. This is all the more the case with [a
decolonial politics of authenticity]{.underline} which, like
intersectionality, [**fixes and essentializes subaltern
culture/identity**, thereby **enabling its
commoditization**]{.underline}.

Consequently, [given the disavowal of any state-like authority or
meaningful materialist analysis or strategy]{.underline}, Mignolo's
[decolonial pluriversality yields to little more than]{.underline} what
decolonial Indigenous scholar Cusicanqui (2012) sees as
'[multiculturalism]{.underline}' (p. 104). Decoloniality may well mount
a critique of the coloniality of power, but in Mignolo's hands, just as
in Crenshaw's, [such critique]{.underline} nonetheless [ends up
**facilitating, not challenging, global capitalism**.
Localism/particularism nicely suits capitalism as much as
coloniality]{.underline}, since [it can be **readily accommodated if not
commodified**]{.underline}. So as with Crenshaw, my claim is that
Mignolo's disavowal of state/political economy and his accompanying
advocacy of the local amounts to an unconscious acceptance of capitalist
multiculturalism. We are thus left with another case of 'fetishistic
disavowal' -- a secret love for the System in spite of an outwardly
biting critique of it[. The global capitalist order sets]{.underline}
([and is allowed to set]{.underline}) [the boundary for decolonial
counterhegemonic critique]{.underline}.

### Alt Fails\-\--Resistance\-\--2AC

#### 'Anticolonial' resistance fetishizes nativism and reproduces problematic binaries. 

Chenchen **Zhang 23**, Assistant Professor of International Relations at
Durham University, Ph.D. from the Free University of Brussels,
"Postcolonial nationalism and the global right," Geoforum, Vol. 144,
August 2023, accessed 06-27-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.geoforum.2023.103824, DG

[For postcolonial critique, the **contemporary abuse** of the
anti-colonial in far-right and ultranationalist projects reminds
us]{.underline} once [again of the dangers of **fetishizing nativist
ideologies** and **thinking of geopolitical macro-categories** such as
North/South and West/non-West **as opposing political
spheres**]{.underline}. Said's warning from 40 years ago remains
relevant today as ever: one cannot respond to "[the tyrannical
conjuncture of colonial power with scholarly Orientalism]{.underline}
simply [by proposing an alliance between nativist sentiment buttressed
some variety of native ideology to combat them]{.underline}" (Said,
1985:103). As Rao has reminded us more recently (2020: 9),
[if]{.underline} "[postcolonial critique is to continue to remain
meaningful]{.underline} in the contemporary world... [\[i\]t must be
attentive to shifts in power, including **those that enable formerly
colonised states to become colonial**]{.underline} in their own rights".
[For the study of the global right, considering the historical
entanglement of nationalism with neoliberalism and authoritarianism in
postcolonial contexts may question the newness of the]{.underline}
so-called "[populist]{.underline}" [backlash against
globalization]{.underline} in the Western core. Many have pointed out
that the far right or [the]{.underline} "[fascist
temptation]{.underline}" [is **not external to liberalism**]{.underline}
(Cooper, 2021, Mondon and Winter, 2020) and [right-wing nationalism and
neoliberalism are not antinomies]{.underline} (Hendrikse, 2018; Kiely,
2021). When we turn to the global trajectories of neoliberalism in the
"rest of the world", it becomes clear that they have long been
overlapped with histories of postcolonial authoritarianism and
nationalism (Watson, 2021). Moreover, [the employment of victimhood
narratives and the appropriation of the anti-colonial rhetoric
are]{.underline} also [characteristic of **right-wing discourses** in
former colonial powers]{.underline} (Al-Ghazzi 2021). Another venue for
future research would be to explore commonalities, differences, and
interconnections in these developments across contexts that are marked
by varying relationships with colonialism and are differently positioned
within global power structures.

#### It can't overcome the binaries they claim produce colonialism. 

Marco **Vieira 19**, Associate Professor of International Relations at
the University of Birmingham, Ph.D. in International Relations from the
London School of Economics and Political Science, "The decolonial
subject and the problem of non-Western authenticity," Postcolonial
Studies, Vol. 22, No. 2, 2019, accessed 06-27-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1080/13688790.2019.1608795, DG

I have argued that in the cases under examination in this article
[decolonial claims for liberation from colonial domination
**cannot**]{.underline} possibly **[erase nor overcome]{.underline}**
what is in fact a variety of [subjective positions that amalgamate
Western and non-Western identity markers in an unstable
relation]{.underline}. I have critically engaged with the work of some
influential Latin American decolonial thinkers as well as IR scholars
who have argued in favour of [retrieving non-Western
subjectivities]{.underline}. The focus was on some core concepts, such
as Mignolo's notions of border thinking and exteriority and Shilliam's
approach to anticolonial affective relationality. I claimed that these
authors have [often reduced]{.underline} their [decolonial theorising to
**binary reifications** of thoroughly negative Western and
**romanticised positive non-Western cultural retentions**]{.underline}.

Ultimately, I claim that the [decolonial approaches]{.underline} I have
examined in this article -- not- withstanding their crucial contribution
to the current theorising from the periphery of global politics -- [are
not adequate for understanding **hybrid post-Western configurations of
subjectivity**]{.underline}. In this regard, I have empirically
[examined the hybrid and paradoxical status of Latin American creole
elites, which combine Indigenous Amerindian with Spanish and European
cultural/cosmological sources into a project of breaking with the
Enlightenment tradition through reconstituting a self-sufficient
post-Western subjectivity]{.underline}. The examination of scholarly
work on Toussaint L'Ouverture's and Simón Bolívar's psychic drives and
political motivations [led to the **confirm**ation of the **inseparable
nature** of their Western and non-Western identity markers. Their
postcolonial subjectivity is]{.underline}, in this regard, [the result
of maintaining an **unstable balance** between imagined representations
of who they were as post-Western entities while striving
for]{.underline} political [liberation still **firmly attached** to an
ever-present modernity/coloniality]{.underline}.

### Alt Fails\-\--Humanism\-\--2AC

#### The alt's refusal of humanism poisons its potential. 

**Boelens et al. 24**, Richard Boelens, Special Professor of the
Political Ecology of Water at the University of Amsterdam Centre for
Latin American Research and Documentation, Personal Professor of Water
Governance and Social Justice at Wageningen University, Ph.D. from
Wageningen University; Lena Hommes, Lecturer and Postdoctoral Researcher
in the Department Geography and the Institute of the Environment at the
University of Girona, Ph.D. from Wageningen University; Jaime
Hoogesteger, Associate Professor of Water Commons and Collectives at
Wageningen University, Ph.D. in Water Governance from Wageningen
University, "Ontological Politics in River Defence Debates: Unpacking
Fields of Contention in Eco-Centric and Non-Human Turns," Water
Alternatives, Vol. 17, No. 3, 2024, accessed 06-28-2025,
https://www.water-alternatives.org/index.php/alldoc/articles/vol17/v17issue3/762-a17-3-4/file,
DG

Catton and Dunlap (1978), among others, have challenged the social
sciences to engage in the urgent task of dismantling the \'human
exceptionalism paradigm\'. Since then, myriad bodies of
transdisciplinary sciences and activism have constructed important
alternatives to the Enlightenment ontology of nature as primordial,
autonomous, and mechanistic, open to human extractivism (cf. Goldman and
Schurman, 2000). In [the non-human turn]{.underline}, this [is often
translated into the need to \'de-centre the human\']{.underline}. We
strongly identify with movements fighting against states' and elites'
exceptionalist claims to having the right to destroy the planet and
accumulate \'natural resources\'. At the same time, however, we warn
activists and academics of the need for caution regarding how
popularised non-human and RoN/RoR policy debates ontologically and
generically tend to de-centre the human. One main reason is that
[human]{.underline} (elite) [groups certainly are exceptional in how
they have radically transformed the human/non-human milieux]{.underline}
(Grusin, 2015; Büscher, 2021). [This demands that we address the **roots
and impacts of this exceptionality, instead of \'ontologising it
away\'**]{.underline}. It also asks for political contestation and the
devising of strategies to make particular classes responsible.
[De-centring the human in policy and activist currents may]{.underline}
tend to [**create binaries** between \'generalised humans\' and
\'generalised non-humans\'. These]{.underline} binaries may [blind us
not just to the exceptionally destructive capacities of some humans and
their inventions, but also to the exceptional suffering of
others]{.underline}. At the same time, it might blind us from
recognising the exceptionally creative capacities that humans have for
reversing the transformation, not just of their own environments, but of
the planetary system as a whole.

[In]{.underline} mainstream [water policies]{.underline}, for instance,
[de-centring humans' exceptional agency has led to **overlooking** the
**complex lived realities of riverine families, livelihoods, meanings,
values, identities, and rights systems**. These policies]{.underline}
have [constructed their own river dwellers and water users, with
identities that conveniently fit the models and policies]{.underline}
(Roa-García, 2014; Hoogesteger, 2015; Hidalgo-Bastidas et al., 2018). In
this way, [they justify **dramatic interventions**]{.underline} (e.g.
Nixon, 2009; Sanchis-Ibor et al., 2017; Lynch, 2019; Aubriot, 2022;
Duarte-Abadía, 2023; Heikkinen, 2024). The [mainstream nature-science-
policy nexus **takes exceptional agency away from \'targeted human
beneficiaries\' and simplifies their ontological diversity in line with
expert notions**. It]{.underline} thereby [produces policy models that
**depoliticise their deeply political choices while dehumanising the
people they affect**]{.underline}. In fact, [they perceive and construct
abstract \'equals\' rather than recognising highly differentiated and
plural realities]{.underline}.

Brackel et al. (2023) provide a common illustration. Communities
affected by the Manila Bay Sustainable Development Master Plan
(elaborated by Dutch and Pilipino \'experts\') were reduced to \"people
in PowerPoint pixels\". These people, who would soon be displaced,
disappeared from view through the \'upscaling perspective\' of
hydro-territorial transformation programs (cf. Melsen et al., 2018).
[Actively de-centring the human, and]{.underline} thereby [constructing
ignorance, helps to \'rationally\' intervene in the complex water world.
This **de-humanises water reality**. Rather than revealing]{.underline}
the [on-the-ground realities, **only governable subjects and objects
appear: an empty space for constructing and ordering rivers, water
users, and identities**]{.underline}.

[This picture is the contrary of what the posthuman]{.underline} and
non-human [turns intend to accomplish]{.underline}. Nevertheless, [in
de-centring the human]{.underline}, many dam removal
[campaigns]{.underline} in North America and Europe tend to
[misrecognise]{.underline} the peasant [communities]{.underline} who
build their livelihoods on the small and medium- sized dam systems --
dams whose removal they project will \'enliven rivers\' and re-centre
nature (for critical reflections, see Sneddon et al., 2017; Drapier et
al., 2023; Harrington and Cantor, 2024). Hommes (2022: 604) shows how
communities along Spain's Mijares river fiercely contest dam removal
that would dry out a local irrigation canal: \"Destroying the Toranes
Dam is damaging this natural, territorial, and social ecosystem in which
we live, it is destroying an organised community \[in reference to the
irrigation community\]\". Another illustration is how RoN policy
advocates may unwittingly collaborate in new enclosures of riverine
commons (Duarte-Abadía et al., 2023). For instance, in the Andean
wetlands of Tungurahua, Ecuador, campesino-indigenous communities deploy
territory-situated conservation practices, but international
conservation agencies threaten them with RoN-based court cases that
could take away their ancestral governance rights if they fail to comply
with PES-based conservation schemes (pers. com. Juan Hidalgo-Bastidas,
10/04/2024). In parallel, RoN and RoR policies de-centring the human may
invisibilise powerful interests. Kinkaid (2019: 561), for instance,
shows how RoN imposition on the Hindu-religious rivers Ganges and Yamuna
by India's right-wing Hindu-nationalist government may lead to the
violent expulsion of Muslim and Dalit river dwellers (labelled
\'unclean\' tannery polluters of the sacred rivers) while leaving the
overall environmental status quo unchallenged.

In general, Büscher posits that, [for the purpose of understanding how
capitalism has made humans alienate themselves from
non-humans]{.underline}, \"\[...\] [it **makes little sense to decentre
the human**]{.underline}\" (2021: 7). [Because we live in increasingly
alienated interrelationships, we have to **re-emphasise
our**]{.underline} (**[human]{.underline}**) [**bonds with and
dependence on the rest of life**. This pays **genuine attention to the
agency, life worlds, and exceptional potential of highly divergent water
commons and collectives**]{.underline}. This [re-centring
would]{.underline} also [challenge any de-contextualised and detached
prescription of the diverse identities and nature of human and non-human
collectives]{.underline}.

### Alt Fails\-\--Blue Humanities\-\--2AC

#### Blue humanities [fail]{.underline}\-\--water cannot represent a [singular ontology]{.underline}, and thinking with the blue [reproduces their impacts]{.underline}. 

Michel **Büch 24**, Postdoctoral Researcher and Producer of the
University Players at the University of Hamburg Institute of English and
American Studies, Ph.D. from the University of Bremen, "'I want a
bath!': On the Depth and Limits of Universalist Liquefaction in Sarah
Ruhl\'s Eurydice," Comparative Drama, Vol. 58, No. 4, Winter 2024,
accessed 06-28-2025, ProQuest, DG

In this essay, I have pitched the engagement with the water metaphor and
materiality in a somewhat phenomenological way against these familiar
and familial readings. I have considered the figure of Eurydice, as well
as the play as a whole, through a kind of blue humanities lens because I
felt there was a lacuna in the way it was discussed, and I could
contribute to the discourse. I have thus attempted to meet it on its own
grounds and have found the discourse on water insightful. The water
metaphor appeared to offer a way into an aesthetic experience beyond
mere recognition for the scholar. However, having sought to address the
play on its own terms, I have been limited by [the]{.underline} very
[limitations]{.underline} that limit the play-and ironically, they
[appear]{.underline} the most [limitless]{.underline}, i.e. the notions
of [fluidity, instability of meaning]{.underline}, and so on. The
effects are consequential; here are some examples.

First, we must recognize that [the metaphor and image of water may
signify differently]{.underline} than Bachelard\'s vision. [The Middle
Passage]{.underline}, for example, [has given rise to an ontology of
being in/as water, where water is **not merely a metaphor** but
signifies **contingent, very concrete death**]{.underline}. The water in
the Atlantic Ocean holds the bodies and specters of those who were
thrown overboard, because insurance payments were higher than their
market value.57 M. NourbeSe Philips Zong! begins with water- but not as
a transcendental, posthumanist vision. Here, [the collapse of meaning
in/as water is a site of horror: **not a liberation** from binary bounds
but a **violent erasure of identity**]{.underline}. In Zong!, [water is
not a space of cleansing or purification but one of trauma, disruption,
and obliteration. Its vastness and depth do not evoke a transcendental
sphere of connection but instead embody an **unfathomable abyss**. The
absence of language to confront the dehumanizing violence and wounds of
enslavement is **not a void to be desired but an unspeakability that
demands reckoning**]{.underline}. Christina [Sharpe uses]{.underline}
the meaning and metaphor of [the]{.underline} \"[wake]{.underline}\" [to
evoke]{.underline}, among other associations, [the literal meanings of
mourning and remembrance and the metaphorical ones associated with the
wake left behind by a]{.underline} ([slave]{.underline}) [ship in
water]{.underline}: the ripple effects of history, where the wake of
past events continues to shape the present. [There is]{.underline}
\"[loss]{.underline}\" [here]{.underline} too, [but of an **entirely
different scale and register**]{.underline} than the themes of Eurydice.
A different example: for African and European audiences, the play\'s
notion of a \"salty lake\" might evoke the Mediterranean Sea and, by
extension, the violent Frontex regime \"securing\" Europe\'s
borders-ironically on the shores of Greece. Swimming courses that help
migrants develop a new relationship to water, after enduring traumatic
experiences, are one symptom of this.58 There is, therefore, [a **clear
distinction** between the imaginary qualities of water signifying death
in a metaphysical sense]{.underline} (which might be accessible only to
minds shaped by white ignorance), [and the]{.underline} very
[**non-metaphorical meanings** of water linked to historically concrete
realities, such as the ontologies of Blackness as social death or the
resonance of violent border regimes]{.underline}.

Second, the [posthumanist fantasies that traverse the blue
humanities]{.underline}-inspired focus on water, all those \"appeals to
move \'beyond the human\' [may]{.underline} actually **[reintroduce the
Eurocentric transcendentalism this movement purports to
disrupt]{.underline}**, particularly [with regard to the historical and
ongoing distributive ordering of race]{.underline}\"59 [To
performatively shed human individualism requires a subjective
capacity]{.underline} that has **[only]{.underline}** **[been available
to whites]{.underline}**. In a racialized modernity, in Wilderson\'s
words, [one needs to belong to the]{.underline} \"[race of
Humanism]{.underline}\"60 in order [to ponder]{.underline} the beyond or
[posthumanism]{.underline} in the first place. [The]{.underline}
somewhat [moralizing attempts to envision humans beyond
race]{.underline} even [provoke **absurd notions**, because for those
inhabiting discursive Blackness, the]{.underline} allegedly [salvaging
prefix is followed by a negation]{.underline}. Posthumanist thought is,
then, not doing the work it claims or desires to do. It is
non-performative, to use Sara Ahmed\'s term. [Its]{.underline}
\"[gestures toward the \'post\' or the \'beyond\' effectively **ignore
praxes of humanity and critiques produced by black people, leading to
awkward bends, incommensurable logics, and difficult
postures**]{.underline}. (Thus, Barack Obamas election did not signal a
postracial era-though the idea appealed to many-and the awkward postures
to pretend it did are now being broadly and violently relaxed.) Here,
[the]{.underline} \"[post]{.underline}\" [instantiates the prior,
reasserting the very structures it (cl)aims to surpass]{.underline}.

The present critique raises several points about the dynamics of
universalism and its often-unexamined Eurocentric underpinnings in the
theater, while at the same time being fully implicated in them. Eurydice
may not be a particularly egregious example of these tendencies, but its
popularity and the discourse it generates reveal how often [white,
Eurocentric perspectives shape the creation, interpretation, and
reception of art]{.underline}-[especially works that aspire
to]{.underline} \"[timelessness]{.underline}\" [or address]{.underline}
\"[universal]{.underline}\" [themes]{.underline}. Theater scholars,
producers, audiences, designers, directors, and actors are left with a
dual responsibility: to reckon with the artistry of works like Eurydice
while critically interrogating their discursive and political
implications. This involves perceiving how such works might perpetuate
racially normative ideas about universality and \"the human\" What
Eurydice does with water and agency invites us to take up this
responsibility, even as it may slips through our fingers.

### Alt Fails\-\--Scholarship\-\--2AC

#### Affirming scholarship is a [hollow gesture]{.underline} that stifles the possibility for material change.

Dorothy **Figueria 8**, Professor of Comparative Literature at the
University of Georgia, Ph.D. in Comparative Studies in Literature from
the University of Chicago, "Critical Background to Poststructuralist
Theories," in *Otherwise Occupied: Pedagogies of Alterity and the
Brahminization of Theory*, pp. 5-14, State University of New York Press,
2008, accessed 06-27-2025, ProQuest, DG

There was indeed a clear paradox inherent in structuralist theory.
[While intellectuals were]{.underline} ostensively [working for change
and developing theoretical weapons]{.underline} to advance a progressive
struggle, [they were]{.underline} at the same time being [seduced by a
paradigm that **stifled all desire for change** and **announced the end
of history**]{.underline} (Dosse 1991:2.72). With a vengeance, May 1968
would exhume history and the subject that structuralism had repressed.
The Annales historians, in particular, were beneficiaries of 1968, since
they were able to transform "History" into "histories" (Veyne 1971).
[History was still discovering the Other]{.underline}. However, [this
Other was not in other lands, but could]{.underline} now [be studied
**within Western civilization** in the form of cultural
histories]{.underline} and the study of mentalités. 4 Anthropology's
Other had become its own past and values. Sometimes [the Other
became]{.underline} just **[the theorist's present]{.underline}**.
Simone de Beauvoir's mandarins of existentialism became Kristeva's
samurai in the new cult of personality. 5 [Criticism turned its
attention to the **discovery of the Self** and its
pleasures]{.underline}, in the case of Barthes, 6 [or]{.underline} in
the case of Kristeva, [its abjection]{.underline}. While it may seem to
be truly the grand return of the repressed subject, it would be a
mistake to think that the Self had not always been present for
theorists.

[It became **increasingly fashionable** to situate onself **at the
edges** of a system of thought]{.underline} in order [to **manipulate**
and **move those edges**. We were no longer interested in the hell of
others, but **the hell in the Self**]{.underline}. In the Archaeology of
Knowledge, Michel [Foucault]{.underline} claimed that man was an
invention of recent date soon to disappear (Foucault 1972:387). He
[championed the destruction of history, faceless writing, and pure
freedom]{.underline} (Foucault 1972:17). The idea was **[to decenter
man]{.underline}** as author, subject, and speaker. Georges Canguilhem's
work on psychology (asking whether it served science or the police) set
the stage for Foucault's work on asylums, prisons, and madness.
[Self-awareness regarding the limits of knowing others]{.underline} now
[became a **precondition for engagement**]{.underline}. Foucault focused
on the periphery and the margins of a system. He analyzed the
development of European civilization as a process of marginalizing
domestic misfits who were kept under surveillance through the
cooperation of social "power" and "knowledge." He further examined
deviation and marginality in Discipline and Punish (1975). The History
of Sexuality (1976) offered a discourse on sex as a form of managerial
power found in censuses and the psychiatrization of deviation.
[The]{.underline} structuralist [ideal]{.underline} had shift[ed from a
scientific method informed by political and cultural pessimism into a
**liberation antitheology celebrating difference**]{.underline},
wherever it might be found.
